936\. Then the BhagavÄ went to the hermitage of Rammaka the brahmin. Now at that time, several bhikkhÅ« were sitting together in Rammaka the brahminâ€™s hermitage, engaged in a Dhamma talk. 937\. â€œGood, bhikkhave! It is fitting for you, bhikkhave, as young men of good families who have gone forth from the home life into homelessness out of faith, that you should sit together engaged in a Dhamma talk. When you are gathered together, bhikkhave, one of two things should be done: either a Dhamma talk, or noble silence.â€

938\. Bhikkhave, there are two kinds of quests: a noble quest and an ignoble quest.

934\. ğ‘€…ğ‘€£ ğ‘€”ğ‘„ ğ‘€ªğ‘€•ğ‘€¯ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€²ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¯ğ‘€¢ğ‘†ğ‘€£ğ‘€ºğ‘€¬ğ‘€ ğ‘€§ğ‘€ºğ‘€¡ğ‘†ğ‘€Ÿğ‘€¸ğ‘€¬ ğ‘€˜ğ‘€­ğ‘€ºğ‘€¢ğ‘†ğ‘€¯ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€§ğ‘€˜ğ‘†ğ‘€™ğ‘€¸ğ‘€ªğ‘€¢ğ‘†ğ‘€¢ğ‘€ ğ‘€§ğ‘€ºğ‘€¡ğ‘†ğ‘€Ÿğ‘€§ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¢ğ‘€§ğ‘€ğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€“ğ‘€¦ğ‘†ğ‘€¢ğ‘„ ğ‘€†ğ‘€¬ğ‘€²ğ‘†ğ‘€«ğ‘€¦ğ‘†ğ‘€¢ğ‘€ ğ‘€†ğ‘€¦ğ‘€¦ğ‘†ğ‘€¤ğ‘€ ğ‘€†ğ‘€«ğ‘€¦ğ‘†ğ‘€¢ğ‘‚ğ‘€²ğ‘€ºâ€”  â€œğ‘€†ğ‘€¬ğ‘€¸ğ‘€«ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¦ğ‘€¦ğ‘†ğ‘€¤, ğ‘€¬ğ‘‚ğ‘€¦ ğ‘€§ğ‘€¼ğ‘€©ğ‘†ğ‘€©ğ‘€¸ğ‘€­ğ‘€¸ğ‘€«ğ‘„ ğ‘€«ğ‘€ºğ‘€•ğ‘€¸ğ‘€­ğ‘€«ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¢ğ‘€¼ğ‘€§ğ‘€¸ğ‘€²ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¤ğ‘„ ğ‘€¢ğ‘‚ğ‘€¦ğ‘€¼ğ‘€§ğ‘€²ğ‘€—ğ‘†ğ‘€“ğ‘€«ğ‘€ºğ‘€²ğ‘†ğ‘€²ğ‘€¸ğ‘€« ğ‘€¤ğ‘€ºğ‘€¯ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¯ğ‘€ºğ‘€³ğ‘€¸ğ‘€­ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¬ğ‘€¸â€ğ‘€¢ğ‘€º. â€œğ‘€ğ‘€¯ğ‘€, ğ‘€ªğ‘€¦ğ‘†ğ‘€¢ğ‘‚â€ğ‘€¢ğ‘€º ğ‘€”ğ‘„ ğ‘€†ğ‘€¬ğ‘€²ğ‘†ğ‘€«ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€†ğ‘€¦ğ‘€¦ğ‘†ğ‘€¤ğ‘„ ğ‘€ªğ‘€•ğ‘€¯ğ‘€¢ğ‘„ ğ‘€§ğ‘€˜ğ‘†ğ‘€˜ğ‘€²ğ‘†ğ‘€²ğ‘„ğ‘€²ğ‘€º. ğ‘€…ğ‘€£ ğ‘€”ğ‘„ ğ‘€ªğ‘€•ğ‘€¯ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€†ğ‘€¬ğ‘€²ğ‘†ğ‘€«ğ‘€¢ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€†ğ‘€¦ğ‘€¦ğ‘†ğ‘€¤ğ‘‚ğ‘€¦ ğ‘€²ğ‘€¤ğ‘†ğ‘€¥ğ‘€ºğ‘€ ğ‘€¬ğ‘‚ğ‘€¦ ğ‘€§ğ‘€¼ğ‘€©ğ‘†ğ‘€©ğ‘€¸ğ‘€­ğ‘€¸ğ‘€«ğ‘„ ğ‘€«ğ‘€ºğ‘€•ğ‘€¸ğ‘€­ğ‘€«ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¢ğ‘€¼ğ‘€§ğ‘€¸ğ‘€²ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¤ğ‘„ ğ‘€¢ğ‘‚ğ‘€¦ğ‘€¼ğ‘€§ğ‘€²ğ‘€—ğ‘†ğ‘€“ğ‘€«ğ‘€º ğ‘€¤ğ‘€ºğ‘€¯ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¯ğ‘€ºğ‘€³ğ‘€¸ğ‘€­ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¬. ğ‘€…ğ‘€£ ğ‘€”ğ‘„ ğ‘€ªğ‘€•ğ‘€¯ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€²ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¬ğ‘€¦ğ‘†ğ‘€³ğ‘€²ğ‘€«ğ‘€¬ğ‘€ ğ‘€§ğ‘€ğ‘€ºğ‘€²ğ‘€®ğ‘†ğ‘€®ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¦ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€¯ğ‘€¼ğ‘€ğ‘†ğ‘€ğ‘€ºğ‘€¢ğ‘„ ğ‘€†ğ‘€¬ğ‘€²ğ‘†ğ‘€«ğ‘€¦ğ‘†ğ‘€¢ğ‘€ ğ‘€†ğ‘€¦ğ‘€¦ğ‘†ğ‘€¤ğ‘€ ğ‘€†ğ‘€«ğ‘€¦ğ‘†ğ‘€¢ğ‘‚ğ‘€²ğ‘€ºâ€”  â€œğ‘€†ğ‘€¬ğ‘€¸ğ‘€«ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¦ğ‘€¦ğ‘†ğ‘€¤, ğ‘€¬ğ‘‚ğ‘€¦ ğ‘€§ğ‘€¼ğ‘€©ğ‘†ğ‘€©ğ‘€“ğ‘„ğ‘€ğ‘†ğ‘€ğ‘€“ğ‘„ ğ‘€¢ğ‘‚ğ‘€¦ğ‘€¼ğ‘€§ğ‘€²ğ‘€—ğ‘†ğ‘€“ğ‘€«ğ‘€ºğ‘€²ğ‘†ğ‘€²ğ‘€¸ğ‘€« ğ‘€•ğ‘€¢ğ‘†ğ‘€¢ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¦ğ‘€º ğ‘€§ğ‘€­ğ‘€ºğ‘€²ğ‘€ºğ‘€œğ‘†ğ‘€˜ğ‘€ºğ‘€¢ğ‘€¼ğ‘€¦ğ‘†â€ğ‘€¢ğ‘€º. â€œğ‘€ğ‘€¯ğ‘€, ğ‘€ªğ‘€¦ğ‘†ğ‘€¢ğ‘‚â€ğ‘€¢ğ‘€º ğ‘€”ğ‘„ ğ‘€†ğ‘€¬ğ‘€²ğ‘†ğ‘€«ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€†ğ‘€¦ğ‘€¦ğ‘†ğ‘€¤ğ‘„ ğ‘€ªğ‘€•ğ‘€¯ğ‘€¢ğ‘„ ğ‘€§ğ‘€˜ğ‘†ğ‘€˜ğ‘€²ğ‘†ğ‘€²ğ‘„ğ‘€²ğ‘€º.

936\. ğ‘€…ğ‘€£ ğ‘€”ğ‘„ ğ‘€ªğ‘€•ğ‘€¯ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€¬ğ‘‚ğ‘€¦ ğ‘€­ğ‘€«ğ‘†ğ‘€«ğ‘€“ğ‘€²ğ‘†ğ‘€² ğ‘€©ğ‘†ğ‘€­ğ‘€¸ğ‘€³ğ‘†ğ‘€«ğ‘€¡ğ‘€²ğ‘†ğ‘€² ğ‘€…ğ‘€²ğ‘†ğ‘€²ğ‘€«ğ‘„ ğ‘€¢ğ‘‚ğ‘€¦ğ‘€¼ğ‘€§ğ‘€²ğ‘€—ğ‘†ğ‘€“ğ‘€«ğ‘€º. ğ‘€¢ğ‘‚ğ‘€¦ ğ‘€”ğ‘„ ğ‘€§ğ‘€¦ ğ‘€²ğ‘€«ğ‘€¬ğ‘‚ğ‘€¦ ğ‘€²ğ‘€«ğ‘†ğ‘€©ğ‘€³ğ‘€¼ğ‘€®ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€ªğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€”ğ‘€½ ğ‘€­ğ‘€«ğ‘†ğ‘€«ğ‘€“ğ‘€²ğ‘†ğ‘€² ğ‘€©ğ‘†ğ‘€­ğ‘€¸ğ‘€³ğ‘†ğ‘€«ğ‘€¡ğ‘€²ğ‘†ğ‘€² ğ‘€…ğ‘€²ğ‘†ğ‘€²ğ‘€«ğ‘‚ ğ‘€¥ğ‘€«ğ‘†ğ‘€«ğ‘€ºğ‘€¬ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€“ğ‘€£ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¬ ğ‘€²ğ‘€¦ğ‘†ğ‘€¦ğ‘€ºğ‘€²ğ‘€ºğ‘€¦ğ‘†ğ‘€¦ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€³ğ‘„ğ‘€¦ğ‘†ğ‘€¢ğ‘€º. ğ‘€…ğ‘€£ ğ‘€”ğ‘„ ğ‘€ªğ‘€•ğ‘€¯ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€©ğ‘€³ğ‘€ºğ‘€¤ğ‘†ğ‘€¯ğ‘€¸ğ‘€­ğ‘€“ğ‘„ğ‘€ğ‘†ğ‘€ğ‘€“ğ‘‚ ğ‘€…ğ‘€ğ‘†ğ‘€ğ‘€¸ğ‘€²ğ‘€º ğ‘€“ğ‘€£ğ‘€¸ğ‘€§ğ‘€­ğ‘€ºğ‘€¬ğ‘„ğ‘€²ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¦ğ‘€ ğ‘€†ğ‘€•ğ‘€«ğ‘€¬ğ‘€«ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¦ğ‘„. ğ‘€…ğ‘€£ ğ‘€”ğ‘„ ğ‘€ªğ‘€•ğ‘€¯ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€“ğ‘€£ğ‘€¸ğ‘€§ğ‘€­ğ‘€ºğ‘€¬ğ‘„ğ‘€²ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¦ğ‘€ ğ‘€¯ğ‘€ºğ‘€¤ğ‘€ºğ‘€¢ğ‘†ğ‘€¯ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€‰ğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€“ğ‘€¸ğ‘€²ğ‘€ºğ‘€¢ğ‘†ğ‘€¯ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€…ğ‘€•ğ‘†ğ‘€•ğ‘€ğ‘€…ğ‘€ ğ‘€†ğ‘€“ğ‘„ğ‘€ğ‘‚ğ‘€²ğ‘€º. ğ‘€¯ğ‘€ºğ‘€¯ğ‘€­ğ‘€ºğ‘€ğ‘€²ğ‘€¼ ğ‘€”ğ‘„ ğ‘€¢ğ‘‚ ğ‘€ªğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€”ğ‘€½ ğ‘€ªğ‘€•ğ‘€¯ğ‘€¢ğ‘„ ğ‘€¤ğ‘†ğ‘€¯ğ‘€¸ğ‘€­ğ‘€. ğ‘€…ğ‘€£ ğ‘€”ğ‘„ ğ‘€ªğ‘€•ğ‘€¯ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€­ğ‘€«ğ‘†ğ‘€«ğ‘€“ğ‘€²ğ‘†ğ‘€² ğ‘€©ğ‘†ğ‘€­ğ‘€¸ğ‘€³ğ‘†ğ‘€«ğ‘€¡ğ‘€²ğ‘†ğ‘€² ğ‘€…ğ‘€²ğ‘†ğ‘€²ğ‘€«ğ‘€ ğ‘€§ğ‘€¯ğ‘€ºğ‘€²ğ‘€ºğ‘€¢ğ‘†ğ‘€¯ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€§ğ‘€œğ‘†ğ‘€œğ‘€¢ğ‘†ğ‘€¢ğ‘‚ ğ‘€†ğ‘€²ğ‘€¦ğ‘‚ ğ‘€¦ğ‘€ºğ‘€²ğ‘€»ğ‘€¤ğ‘€º. ğ‘€¦ğ‘€ºğ‘€²ğ‘€šğ‘†ğ‘€š ğ‘€”ğ‘„ ğ‘€ªğ‘€•ğ‘€¯ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€ªğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€”ğ‘€½ ğ‘€†ğ‘€«ğ‘€¦ğ‘†ğ‘€¢ğ‘‚ğ‘€²ğ‘€ºâ€”  â€œğ‘€“ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¬ ğ‘€¦ğ‘€¼ğ‘€¢ğ‘†ğ‘€£, ğ‘€ªğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€”ğ‘€¯ğ‘‚, ğ‘€ğ‘€¢ğ‘€­ğ‘€³ğ‘€º ğ‘€“ğ‘€£ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¬ ğ‘€²ğ‘€¦ğ‘†ğ‘€¦ğ‘€ºğ‘€²ğ‘€ºğ‘€¦ğ‘†ğ‘€¦ğ‘€¸? ğ‘€“ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€˜ ğ‘€§ğ‘€¦ ğ‘€¯ğ‘„ ğ‘€…ğ‘€¦ğ‘†ğ‘€¢ğ‘€­ğ‘€¸ğ‘€“ğ‘€£ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€¯ğ‘€ºğ‘€§ğ‘†ğ‘€§ğ‘€“ğ‘€¢ğ‘€¸â€ğ‘€¢ğ‘€º? â€œğ‘€ªğ‘€•ğ‘€¯ğ‘€¦ğ‘†ğ‘€¢ğ‘€«ğ‘‚ğ‘€¯ ğ‘€”ğ‘„ ğ‘€¦ğ‘„, ğ‘€ªğ‘€¦ğ‘†ğ‘€¢ğ‘‚, ğ‘€†ğ‘€­ğ‘€©ğ‘†ğ‘€ª ğ‘€¥ğ‘€«ğ‘†ğ‘€«ğ‘€» ğ‘€“ğ‘€£ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€¯ğ‘€ºğ‘€§ğ‘†ğ‘€§ğ‘€“ğ‘€¢ğ‘€¸, ğ‘€…ğ‘€£ ğ‘€ªğ‘€•ğ‘€¯ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€…ğ‘€¦ğ‘€¼ğ‘€§ğ‘†ğ‘€§ğ‘€¢ğ‘†ğ‘€¢ğ‘„â€ğ‘€¢ğ‘€º.

937\. â€œğ‘€²ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¥ğ‘€¼, ğ‘€ªğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€”ğ‘€¯ğ‘‚. ğ‘€ğ‘€¢ğ‘€ ğ‘€”ğ‘„, ğ‘€ªğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€”ğ‘€¯ğ‘‚, ğ‘€¢ğ‘€¼ğ‘€«ğ‘†ğ‘€³ğ‘€¸ğ‘€“ğ‘€ ğ‘€§ğ‘€¢ğ‘€ºğ‘€­ğ‘€½ğ‘€§ğ‘€ ğ‘€“ğ‘€¼ğ‘€®ğ‘€§ğ‘€¼ğ‘€¢ğ‘†ğ‘€¢ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¦ğ‘€ ğ‘€²ğ‘€¤ğ‘†ğ‘€¥ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€…ğ‘€•ğ‘€¸ğ‘€­ğ‘€²ğ‘†ğ‘€«ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€…ğ‘€¦ğ‘€•ğ‘€¸ğ‘€­ğ‘€ºğ‘€¬ğ‘€ ğ‘€§ğ‘€©ğ‘†ğ‘€©ğ‘€šğ‘€ºğ‘€¢ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¦ğ‘€ ğ‘€¬ğ‘€ ğ‘€¢ğ‘€¼ğ‘€«ğ‘†ğ‘€³ğ‘‚ ğ‘€¥ğ‘€«ğ‘†ğ‘€«ğ‘€ºğ‘€¬ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€“ğ‘€£ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¬ ğ‘€²ğ‘€¦ğ‘†ğ‘€¦ğ‘€ºğ‘€²ğ‘€»ğ‘€¤ğ‘‚ğ‘€¬ğ‘†ğ‘€¬ğ‘€¸ğ‘€£. ğ‘€²ğ‘€¦ğ‘†ğ‘€¦ğ‘€ºğ‘€§ğ‘€¢ğ‘€ºğ‘€¢ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¦ğ‘€ ğ‘€¯ğ‘„, ğ‘€ªğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€”ğ‘€¯ğ‘‚, ğ‘€¤ğ‘†ğ‘€¯ğ‘€¬ğ‘€ ğ‘€“ğ‘€­ğ‘€¡ğ‘€»ğ‘€¬ğ‘€â€”  ğ‘€¥ğ‘€«ğ‘†ğ‘€«ğ‘€» ğ‘€¯ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€“ğ‘€£ğ‘€¸, ğ‘€…ğ‘€­ğ‘€ºğ‘€¬ğ‘„ ğ‘€¯ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€¢ğ‘€¼ğ‘€¡ğ‘†ğ‘€³ğ‘€»ğ‘€ªğ‘€¸ğ‘€¯ğ‘„.

## The Ignoble Quest

<Tabs syncKey="paliquote">
<TabItem label="My Translation">
939\. And what, bhikkhave, is the ignoble quest? Here, bhikkhave, someone, being subject to birth himself, seeks what is subject to birth; being subject to aging himself, he seeks what is subject to aging; being subject to sickness himself, he seeks what is subject to sickness; being subject to death himself, he seeks what is subject to death; being subject to sorrow himself, he seeks what is subject to sorrow; being subject to defilement himself, he seeks what is subject to defilement.

940\. And what, bhikkhave, do you call subject to birth? Children and spouses, bhikkhave, are subject to birth; male and female slaves are subject to birth; goats and sheep are subject to birth; chickens and pigs are subject to birth; elephants, cattle, horses, and mares are subject to birth; gold and silver are subject to birth. These, bhikkhave, are acquisitions subject to birth. Entangled, infatuated, and engrossed in these, being subject to birth himself, he seeks what is subject to birth.

941\. And what, bhikkhave, do you call subject to aging? Children and spouses, bhikkhave, are subject to aging; male and female slaves are subject to aging; goats and sheep are subject to aging; chickens and pigs are subject to aging; elephants, cattle, horses, and mares are subject to aging; gold and silver are subject to aging. These, bhikkhave, are acquisitions subject to aging. Entangled, infatuated, and engrossed in these, being subject to aging himself, he seeks what is subject to aging.

942\. And what, bhikkhave, do you call subject to sickness? Children and spouses, bhikkhave, are subject to sickness; male and female slaves are subject to sickness; goats and sheep are subject to sickness; chickens and pigs are subject to sickness; elephants, cattle, horses, and mares are subject to sickness. These, bhikkhave, are acquisitions subject to sickness. Entangled, infatuated, and engrossed in these, being subject to sickness himself, he seeks what is subject to sickness.

943\. And what, bhikkhave, do you call subject to death? Children and spouses, bhikkhave, are subject to death; male and female slaves are subject to death; goats and sheep are subject to death; chickens and pigs are subject to death; elephants, cattle, horses, and mares are subject to death. These, bhikkhave, are acquisitions subject to death. Entangled, infatuated, and engrossed in these, being subject to death himself, he seeks what is subject to death.

944\. And what, bhikkhave, do you call subject to sorrow? Children and spouses, bhikkhave, are subject to sorrow; male and female slaves are subject to sorrow; goats and sheep are subject to sorrow; chickens and pigs are subject to sorrow; elephants, cattle, horses, and mares are subject to sorrow. These, bhikkhave, are acquisitions subject to sorrow. Entangled, infatuated, and engrossed in these, being subject to sorrow himself, he seeks what is subject to sorrow.

945\. And what, bhikkhave, do you call subject to defilement? Children and spouses, bhikkhave, are subject to defilement; male and female slaves are subject to defilement; goats and sheep are subject to defilement; chickens and pigs are subject to defilement; elephants, cattle, horses, and mares are subject to defilement; gold and silver are subject to defilement. These, bhikkhave, are acquisitions subject to defilement. Entangled, infatuated, and engrossed in these, being subject to defilement himself, he seeks what is subject to defilement. This, bhikkhave, is the ignoble quest.
</TabItem>

</Tabs>

## The Noble Quest

<Tabs syncKey="paliquote">
<TabItem label="My Translation">
  946\. And what, bhikkhave, is the noble quest? Here, bhikkhave, someone, being subject to birth himself, having seen the drawback in what is subject to birth, seeks the unborn, unsurpassed security from bondage, Nibbana; being subject to aging himself, having seen the drawback in what is subject to aging, seeks the unaging, unsurpassed security from bondage, Nibbana; being subject to sickness himself, having seen the drawback in what is subject to sickness, seeks the unailing, unsurpassed security from bondage, Nibbana; being subject to death himself, having seen the drawback in what is subject to death, seeks the undying, unsurpassed security from bondage, Nibbana; being subject to sorrow himself, having seen the drawback in what is subject to sorrow, seeks the sorrowless, unsurpassed security from bondage, Nibbana; being subject to defilement himself, having seen the drawback in what is subject to defilement, seeks the undefiled, unsurpassed security from bondage, Nibbana. This, bhikkhave, is the noble quest.
</TabItem>

<TabItem label="PÄá¸·i (Roman IAST)">
946\. KatamÄ ca, bhikkhave, ariyÄ pariyesanÄ? Idha, bhikkhave, ekacco attanÄ jÄtidhammo samÄno jÄtidhamme ÄdÄ«navaá¹ƒ viditvÄ ajÄtaá¹ƒ anuttaraá¹ƒ yogakkhemaá¹ƒ nibbÄnaá¹ƒ pariyesati, attanÄ jarÄdhammo samÄno jarÄdhamme ÄdÄ«navaá¹ƒ viditvÄ ajaraá¹ƒ anuttaraá¹ƒ yogakkhemaá¹ƒ nibbÄnaá¹ƒ pariyesati, attanÄ byÄdhidhammo samÄno byÄdhidhamme ÄdÄ«navaá¹ƒ viditvÄ abyÄdhiá¹ƒ anuttaraá¹ƒ yogakkhemaá¹ƒ nibbÄnaá¹ƒ pariyesati, attanÄ maraá¹‡adhammo samÄno maraá¹‡adhamme ÄdÄ«navaá¹ƒ viditvÄ amataá¹ƒ anuttaraá¹ƒ yogakkhemaá¹ƒ nibbÄnaá¹ƒ pariyesati, attanÄ sokadhammo samÄno sokadhamme ÄdÄ«navaá¹ƒ viditvÄ asokaá¹ƒ anuttaraá¹ƒ yogakkhemaá¹ƒ nibbÄnaá¹ƒ pariyesati, attanÄ saá¹ƒkilesadhammo samÄno saá¹ƒkilesadhamme ÄdÄ«navaá¹ƒ viditvÄ asaá¹ƒkiliá¹­á¹­haá¹ƒ anuttaraá¹ƒ yogakkhemaá¹ƒ nibbÄnaá¹ƒ pariyesati. Ayaá¹ƒ, bhikkhave, ariyÄ pariyesanÄ.

## Renunciation

<Tabs syncKey="paliquote">
<TabItem label="My Translation">
  947\. Bhikkhave, I too, before my full awakening, while still an unawakened Bodhisatta, being subject to birth myself, sought what is subject to birth; being subject to aging myself, I sought what is subject to aging; being subject to sickness myself, I sought what is subject to sickness; being subject to death myself, I sought what is subject to death; being subject to sorrow myself, I sought what is subject to sorrow; being subject to defilement myself, I sought what is subject to defilement. Then, bhikkhave, this thought occurred to me: â€˜What if I, being subject to birth myself, seek what is subject to birth; being subject to aging myself, seek what is subject to aging; being subject to sickness myself, seek what is subject to sickness; being subject to death myself, seek what is subject to death; being subject to sorrow myself, seek what is subject to sorrow; being subject to defilement myself, seek what is subject to defilement? How would it be if I, being subject to birth myself, having seen the drawback in what is subject to birth, were to seek the unborn, unsurpassed security from bondage, Nibbana; being subject to aging myself, having seen the drawback in what is subject to aging, were to seek the unaging, unsurpassed security from bondage, Nibbana; being subject to sickness myself, having seen the drawback in what is subject to sickness, were to seek the unailing, unsurpassed security from bondage, Nibbana; being subject to death myself, having seen the drawback in what is subject to death, were to seek the undying, unsurpassed security from bondage, Nibbana; being subject to sorrow myself, having seen the drawback in what is subject to sorrow, were to seek the sorrowless, unsurpassed security from bondage, Nibbana; being subject to defilement myself, having seen the drawback in what is subject to defilement, were to seek the undefiled, unsurpassed security from bondage, Nibbana?â€™
</TabItem>

<TabItem label="PÄá¸·i (Roman IAST)">

## Ä€á¸·Ära KÄlÄma

<Tabs syncKey="paliquote">
<TabItem label="My Translation">
948\. So, bhikkhave, at a later time, while I was still young, with jet-black hair, endowed with the prime of youth, in the first stage of lifeâ€”though my unwilling mother and father wept with tear-filled facesâ€”having shaved off my hair and beard, and having put on saffron robes, I went forth from the home life into homelessness. Having thus gone forth, seeking what is wholesome, seeking the unsurpassed state of supreme peace, I approached where Ä€á¸·Ära KÄlÄma was. Having approached, I said to Ä€á¸·Ära KÄlÄma: â€œI wish, Ävuso KÄlÄma, to lead the holy life in this Dhamma and discipline.â€ When I said this, bhikkhave, Ä€á¸·Ära KÄlÄma said to me: â€œMay the venerable one stay; such is this Dhamma that a wise person, in no long time, having realized and personally experienced the doctrine of his own teacher, may abide.â€ So, bhikkhave, quickly, in no long time, I learned that Dhamma. So, bhikkhave, merely by lip-service, by mere verbal declaration, I would speak of the doctrine of knowledge and the doctrine of elders, and I would claim, â€˜I know, I see,â€™ and so would others. Then, bhikkhave, this thought occurred to me: â€˜Ä€á¸·Ära KÄlÄma does not merely declare this Dhamma as something he dwells in, having realized and personally experienced it by mere faith; surely Ä€á¸·Ära KÄlÄma dwells in this Dhamma knowing and seeing it.â€™

949\. Then, bhikkhave, I approached where Ä€á¸·Ära KÄlÄma was; having approached, I said to Ä€á¸·Ära KÄlÄma: â€˜To what extent, Ävuso KÄlÄma, do you declare this Dhamma as something you dwell in, having realized and personally experienced it?â€™ When I said this, bhikkhave, Ä€á¸·Ära KÄlÄma declared the dimension of nothingness. Then, bhikkhave, this thought occurred to me: â€˜It is not only Ä€á¸·Ära KÄlÄma who has faith; I also have faith; it is not only Ä€á¸·Ära KÄlÄma who has energy; I also have energy; it is not only Ä€á¸·Ära KÄlÄma who has mindfulness; I also have mindfulness; it is not only Ä€á¸·Ära KÄlÄma who has concentration; I also have concentration; it is not only Ä€á¸·Ära KÄlÄma who has wisdom; I also have wisdom. What if I were to strive for the realization of that Dhamma which Ä€á¸·Ära KÄlÄma declares he dwells in, having realized and personally experienced it?â€™ So, bhikkhave, quickly, in no long time, having realized and personally experienced that Dhamma, I dwelt in it.

950\. Then, bhikkhave, I approached where Ä€á¸·Ära KÄlÄma was; having approached, I said to Ä€á¸·Ära KÄlÄma:

951\. â€˜To what extent, Ävuso KÄlÄma, do you declare this Dhamma as something you dwell in, having realized and personally experienced it?â€™

952\. â€˜To this extent, Ävuso, do I declare this Dhamma as something I dwell in, having realized and personally experienced it.â€™

953\. â€˜I too, Ävuso, to this extent dwell in this Dhamma, having realized and personally experienced it.â€™

954\. â€˜It is a gain for us, Ävuso, it is a great gain for us, that we see such a venerable one as a co-practitioner. Thus, the Dhamma that I declare, having realized and personally experienced it, that Dhamma you dwell in, having realized and personally experienced it. The Dhamma that you dwell in, having realized and personally experienced it, that Dhamma I declare, having realized and personally experienced it. Thus, the Dhamma that I know, that Dhamma you know; the Dhamma that you know, that Dhamma I know. Thus, as I am, so are you; as you are, so am I. Come now, Ävuso, let us both, being present, lead this company.â€™

955\. Thus, bhikkhave, Ä€á¸·Ära KÄlÄma, being my teacher, placed me, his pupil, on the same level as himself, and honored me with great honor. Then, bhikkhave, this thought occurred to me: â€˜This Dhamma does not lead to disenchantment, nor to dispassion, nor to cessation, nor to tranquility, nor to direct knowledge, nor to full awakening, nor to Nibbana; it leads only to rebirth in the dimension of nothingness.â€™ So, bhikkhave, not valuing that Dhamma, I became disenchanted with that Dhamma and departed.

ğ‘€ğ‘€¯ğ‘€ ğ‘€¯ğ‘€¼ğ‘€¢ğ‘†ğ‘€¢ğ‘‚, ğ‘€ªğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€”ğ‘€¯ğ‘‚, ğ‘€†ğ‘€ğ‘€†ğ‘€­ğ‘„ ğ‘€“ğ‘€¸ğ‘€®ğ‘€¸ğ‘€«ğ‘„ ğ‘€†ğ‘€“ğ‘€ºğ‘€œğ‘†ğ‘€˜ğ‘€œğ‘†ğ‘€œğ‘€¸ğ‘€¬ğ‘€¢ğ‘€¦ğ‘€ ğ‘€§ğ‘€¯ğ‘‚ğ‘€¤ğ‘‚ğ‘€²ğ‘€º. ğ‘€¢ğ‘€²ğ‘†ğ‘€² ğ‘€«ğ‘€¬ğ‘†ğ‘€³ğ‘€, ğ‘€ªğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€”ğ‘€¯ğ‘‚, ğ‘€ğ‘€¢ğ‘€¤ğ‘€³ğ‘„ğ‘€²ğ‘€ºâ€”  â€˜ğ‘€¦ ğ‘€”ğ‘„ ğ‘€†ğ‘€ğ‘€†ğ‘€­ğ‘€²ğ‘†ğ‘€²ğ‘‚ğ‘€¯ ğ‘€“ğ‘€¸ğ‘€®ğ‘€¸ğ‘€«ğ‘€²ğ‘†ğ‘€² ğ‘€…ğ‘€¢ğ‘†ğ‘€£ğ‘€º ğ‘€²ğ‘€¤ğ‘†ğ‘€¥ğ‘€¸, ğ‘€«ğ‘€¬ğ‘†ğ‘€³ğ‘€«ğ‘†ğ‘€§ğ‘€¢ğ‘†ğ‘€£ğ‘€º ğ‘€²ğ‘€¤ğ‘†ğ‘€¥ğ‘€¸; ğ‘€¦ ğ‘€”ğ‘„ ğ‘€†ğ‘€ğ‘€†ğ‘€­ğ‘€²ğ‘†ğ‘€²ğ‘‚ğ‘€¯ ğ‘€“ğ‘€¸ğ‘€®ğ‘€¸ğ‘€«ğ‘€²ğ‘†ğ‘€² ğ‘€…ğ‘€¢ğ‘†ğ‘€£ğ‘€º ğ‘€¯ğ‘€»ğ‘€­ğ‘€ºğ‘€¬ğ‘€, ğ‘€«ğ‘€¬ğ‘†ğ‘€³ğ‘€«ğ‘†ğ‘€§ğ‘€¢ğ‘†ğ‘€£ğ‘€º ğ‘€¯ğ‘€»ğ‘€­ğ‘€ºğ‘€¬ğ‘€; ğ‘€¦ ğ‘€”ğ‘„ ğ‘€†ğ‘€ğ‘€†ğ‘€­ğ‘€²ğ‘†ğ‘€²ğ‘‚ğ‘€¯ ğ‘€“ğ‘€¸ğ‘€®ğ‘€¸ğ‘€«ğ‘€²ğ‘†ğ‘€² ğ‘€…ğ‘€¢ğ‘†ğ‘€£ğ‘€º ğ‘€²ğ‘€¢ğ‘€º, ğ‘€«ğ‘€¬ğ‘†ğ‘€³ğ‘€«ğ‘†ğ‘€§ğ‘€¢ğ‘†ğ‘€£ğ‘€º ğ‘€²ğ‘€¢ğ‘€º; ğ‘€¦ ğ‘€”ğ‘„ ğ‘€†ğ‘€ğ‘€†ğ‘€­ğ‘€²ğ‘†ğ‘€²ğ‘‚ğ‘€¯ ğ‘€“ğ‘€¸ğ‘€®ğ‘€¸ğ‘€«ğ‘€²ğ‘†ğ‘€² ğ‘€…ğ‘€¢ğ‘†ğ‘€£ğ‘€º ğ‘€²ğ‘€«ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¥ğ‘€º, ğ‘€«ğ‘€¬ğ‘†ğ‘€³ğ‘€«ğ‘†ğ‘€§ğ‘€¢ğ‘†ğ‘€£ğ‘€º ğ‘€²ğ‘€«ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¥ğ‘€º; ğ‘€¦ ğ‘€”ğ‘„ ğ‘€†ğ‘€ğ‘€†ğ‘€­ğ‘€²ğ‘†ğ‘€²ğ‘‚ğ‘€¯ ğ‘€“ğ‘€¸ğ‘€®ğ‘€¸ğ‘€«ğ‘€²ğ‘†ğ‘€² ğ‘€…ğ‘€¢ğ‘†ğ‘€£ğ‘€º ğ‘€§ğ‘€œğ‘†ğ‘€œğ‘€¸, ğ‘€«ğ‘€¬ğ‘†ğ‘€³ğ‘€«ğ‘†ğ‘€§ğ‘€¢ğ‘†ğ‘€£ğ‘€º ğ‘€§ğ‘€œğ‘†ğ‘€œğ‘€¸. ğ‘€¬ğ‘€¦ğ‘†ğ‘€¦ğ‘€½ğ‘€¦ğ‘€¸ğ‘€³ğ‘€ ğ‘€¬ğ‘€ ğ‘€¥ğ‘€«ğ‘†ğ‘€«ğ‘€ ğ‘€†ğ‘€ğ‘€†ğ‘€­ğ‘„ ğ‘€“ğ‘€¸ğ‘€®ğ‘€¸ğ‘€«ğ‘„ ğ‘€²ğ‘€¬ğ‘€ ğ‘€…ğ‘€ªğ‘€ºğ‘€œğ‘†ğ‘€œğ‘€¸ ğ‘€²ğ‘€˜ğ‘†ğ‘€™ğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘€¢ğ‘†ğ‘€¯ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€‰ğ‘€§ğ‘€²ğ‘€«ğ‘†ğ‘€§ğ‘€šğ‘†ğ‘€š ğ‘€¯ğ‘€ºğ‘€³ğ‘€­ğ‘€¸ğ‘€«ğ‘€»ğ‘€¢ğ‘€º ğ‘€§ğ‘€¯ğ‘‚ğ‘€¤ğ‘‚ğ‘€¢ğ‘€º, ğ‘€¢ğ‘€²ğ‘†ğ‘€² ğ‘€¥ğ‘€«ğ‘†ğ‘€«ğ‘€²ğ‘†ğ‘€² ğ‘€²ğ‘€˜ğ‘†ğ‘€™ğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘€ºğ‘€­ğ‘€ºğ‘€¬ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¬ ğ‘€§ğ‘€¤ğ‘€³ğ‘‚ğ‘€¬ğ‘†ğ‘€¬ğ‘€¦ğ‘†â€™ğ‘€¢ğ‘€º. ğ‘€²ğ‘„ ğ‘€”ğ‘„ ğ‘€…ğ‘€³ğ‘€, ğ‘€ªğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€”ğ‘€¯ğ‘‚, ğ‘€¦ğ‘€˜ğ‘€ºğ‘€­ğ‘€²ğ‘†ğ‘€²ğ‘‚ğ‘€¯ ğ‘€”ğ‘€ºğ‘€§ğ‘†ğ‘€§ğ‘€«ğ‘‚ğ‘€¯ ğ‘€¢ğ‘€ ğ‘€¥ğ‘€«ğ‘†ğ‘€«ğ‘€ ğ‘€²ğ‘€¬ğ‘€ ğ‘€…ğ‘€ªğ‘€ºğ‘€œğ‘†ğ‘€œğ‘€¸ ğ‘€²ğ‘€˜ğ‘†ğ‘€™ğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘€¢ğ‘†ğ‘€¯ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€‰ğ‘€§ğ‘€²ğ‘€«ğ‘†ğ‘€§ğ‘€šğ‘†ğ‘€š ğ‘€¯ğ‘€ºğ‘€³ğ‘€¸ğ‘€²ğ‘€ºğ‘€.

950\. ğ‘€…ğ‘€£ ğ‘€”ğ‘†ğ‘€¯ğ‘€¸ğ‘€³ğ‘€, ğ‘€ªğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€”ğ‘€¯ğ‘‚, ğ‘€¬ğ‘‚ğ‘€¦ ğ‘€†ğ‘€ğ‘€†ğ‘€­ğ‘„ ğ‘€“ğ‘€¸ğ‘€®ğ‘€¸ğ‘€«ğ‘„ ğ‘€¢ğ‘‚ğ‘€¦ğ‘€¼ğ‘€§ğ‘€²ğ‘€—ğ‘†ğ‘€“ğ‘€«ğ‘€ºğ‘€; ğ‘€‰ğ‘€§ğ‘€²ğ‘€—ğ‘†ğ‘€“ğ‘€«ğ‘€ºğ‘€¢ğ‘†ğ‘€¯ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€†ğ‘€ğ‘€†ğ‘€­ğ‘€ ğ‘€“ğ‘€¸ğ‘€®ğ‘€¸ğ‘€«ğ‘€ ğ‘€ğ‘€¢ğ‘€¤ğ‘€¯ğ‘„ğ‘€˜ğ‘€â€”

951\. â€˜ğ‘€ğ‘€¢ğ‘†ğ‘€¢ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¯ğ‘€¢ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€¦ğ‘„, ğ‘€†ğ‘€¯ğ‘€¼ğ‘€²ğ‘„ ğ‘€“ğ‘€¸ğ‘€®ğ‘€¸ğ‘€«, ğ‘€‡ğ‘€«ğ‘€ ğ‘€¥ğ‘€«ğ‘†ğ‘€«ğ‘€ ğ‘€²ğ‘€¬ğ‘€ ğ‘€…ğ‘€ªğ‘€ºğ‘€œğ‘†ğ‘€œğ‘€¸ ğ‘€²ğ‘€˜ğ‘†ğ‘€™ğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘€¢ğ‘†ğ‘€¯ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€‰ğ‘€§ğ‘€²ğ‘€«ğ‘†ğ‘€§ğ‘€šğ‘†ğ‘€š ğ‘€§ğ‘€¯ğ‘‚ğ‘€¤ğ‘‚ğ‘€²ğ‘€»â€™ğ‘€¢ğ‘€º?

952\. â€˜ğ‘€ğ‘€¢ğ‘†ğ‘€¢ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¯ğ‘€¢ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€”ğ‘„ ğ‘€…ğ‘€³ğ‘€, ğ‘€†ğ‘€¯ğ‘€¼ğ‘€²ğ‘„, ğ‘€‡ğ‘€«ğ‘€ ğ‘€¥ğ‘€«ğ‘†ğ‘€«ğ‘€ ğ‘€²ğ‘€¬ğ‘€ ğ‘€…ğ‘€ªğ‘€ºğ‘€œğ‘†ğ‘€œğ‘€¸ ğ‘€²ğ‘€˜ğ‘†ğ‘€™ğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘€¢ğ‘†ğ‘€¯ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€‰ğ‘€§ğ‘€²ğ‘€«ğ‘†ğ‘€§ğ‘€šğ‘†ğ‘€š ğ‘€§ğ‘€¯ğ‘‚ğ‘€¤ğ‘‚ğ‘€«ğ‘€»â€™ğ‘€¢ğ‘€º.

954\. â€˜ğ‘€®ğ‘€¸ğ‘€ªğ‘€¸ ğ‘€¦ğ‘„, ğ‘€†ğ‘€¯ğ‘€¼ğ‘€²ğ‘„, ğ‘€²ğ‘€¼ğ‘€®ğ‘€¤ğ‘†ğ‘€¥ğ‘€ ğ‘€¦ğ‘„, ğ‘€†ğ‘€¯ğ‘€¼ğ‘€²ğ‘„, ğ‘€¬ğ‘‚ ğ‘€«ğ‘€¬ğ‘€ ğ‘€†ğ‘€¬ğ‘€²ğ‘†ğ‘€«ğ‘€¦ğ‘†ğ‘€¢ğ‘€ ğ‘€¢ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¤ğ‘€ºğ‘€²ğ‘€ ğ‘€²ğ‘€©ğ‘†ğ‘€­ğ‘€³ğ‘†ğ‘€«ğ‘€˜ğ‘€¸ğ‘€­ğ‘€ºğ‘€ ğ‘€§ğ‘€²ğ‘†ğ‘€²ğ‘€¸ğ‘€«. ğ‘€‡ğ‘€¢ğ‘€º ğ‘€¬ğ‘€¸ğ‘€³ğ‘€ ğ‘€¥ğ‘€«ğ‘†ğ‘€«ğ‘€ ğ‘€²ğ‘€¬ğ‘€ ğ‘€…ğ‘€ªğ‘€ºğ‘€œğ‘†ğ‘€œğ‘€¸ ğ‘€²ğ‘€˜ğ‘†ğ‘€™ğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘€¢ğ‘†ğ‘€¯ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€‰ğ‘€§ğ‘€²ğ‘€«ğ‘†ğ‘€§ğ‘€šğ‘†ğ‘€š ğ‘€§ğ‘€¯ğ‘‚ğ‘€¤ğ‘‚ğ‘€«ğ‘€º ğ‘€¢ğ‘€ ğ‘€¢ğ‘†ğ‘€¯ğ‘€ ğ‘€¥ğ‘€«ğ‘†ğ‘€«ğ‘€ ğ‘€²ğ‘€¬ğ‘€ ğ‘€…ğ‘€ªğ‘€ºğ‘€œğ‘†ğ‘€œğ‘€¸ ğ‘€²ğ‘€˜ğ‘†ğ‘€™ğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘€¢ğ‘†ğ‘€¯ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€‰ğ‘€§ğ‘€²ğ‘€«ğ‘†ğ‘€§ğ‘€šğ‘†ğ‘€š ğ‘€¯ğ‘€ºğ‘€³ğ‘€­ğ‘€²ğ‘€º. ğ‘€¬ğ‘€ ğ‘€¢ğ‘†ğ‘€¯ğ‘€ ğ‘€¥ğ‘€«ğ‘†ğ‘€«ğ‘€ ğ‘€²ğ‘€¬ğ‘€ ğ‘€…ğ‘€ªğ‘€ºğ‘€œğ‘†ğ‘€œğ‘€¸ ğ‘€²ğ‘€˜ğ‘†ğ‘€™ğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘€¢ğ‘†ğ‘€¯ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€‰ğ‘€§ğ‘€²ğ‘€«ğ‘†ğ‘€§ğ‘€šğ‘†ğ‘€š ğ‘€¯ğ‘€ºğ‘€³ğ‘€­ğ‘€²ğ‘€º ğ‘€¢ğ‘€«ğ‘€³ğ‘€ ğ‘€¥ğ‘€«ğ‘†ğ‘€«ğ‘€ ğ‘€²ğ‘€¬ğ‘€ ğ‘€…ğ‘€ªğ‘€ºğ‘€œğ‘†ğ‘€œğ‘€¸ ğ‘€²ğ‘€˜ğ‘†ğ‘€™ğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘€¢ğ‘†ğ‘€¯ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€‰ğ‘€§ğ‘€²ğ‘€«ğ‘†ğ‘€§ğ‘€šğ‘†ğ‘€š ğ‘€§ğ‘€¯ğ‘‚ğ‘€¤ğ‘‚ğ‘€«ğ‘€º. ğ‘€‡ğ‘€¢ğ‘€º ğ‘€¬ğ‘€¸ğ‘€³ğ‘€ ğ‘€¥ğ‘€«ğ‘†ğ‘€«ğ‘€ ğ‘€šğ‘€¸ğ‘€¦ğ‘€¸ğ‘€«ğ‘€º ğ‘€¢ğ‘€ ğ‘€¢ğ‘†ğ‘€¯ğ‘€ ğ‘€¥ğ‘€«ğ‘†ğ‘€«ğ‘€ ğ‘€šğ‘€¸ğ‘€¦ğ‘€¸ğ‘€²ğ‘€º, ğ‘€¬ğ‘€ ğ‘€¢ğ‘†ğ‘€¯ğ‘€ ğ‘€¥ğ‘€«ğ‘†ğ‘€«ğ‘€ ğ‘€šğ‘€¸ğ‘€¦ğ‘€¸ğ‘€²ğ‘€º ğ‘€¢ğ‘€«ğ‘€³ğ‘€ ğ‘€¥ğ‘€«ğ‘†ğ‘€«ğ‘€ ğ‘€šğ‘€¸ğ‘€¦ğ‘€¸ğ‘€«ğ‘€º. ğ‘€‡ğ‘€¢ğ‘€º ğ‘€¬ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¤ğ‘€ºğ‘€²ğ‘„ ğ‘€…ğ‘€³ğ‘€ ğ‘€¢ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¤ğ‘€ºğ‘€²ğ‘„ ğ‘€¢ğ‘€¼ğ‘€¯ğ‘€, ğ‘€¬ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¤ğ‘€ºğ‘€²ğ‘„ ğ‘€¢ğ‘€¼ğ‘€¯ğ‘€ ğ‘€¢ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¤ğ‘€ºğ‘€²ğ‘„ ğ‘€…ğ‘€³ğ‘€. ğ‘€ğ‘€³ğ‘€º ğ‘€¤ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¦ğ‘€º, ğ‘€†ğ‘€¯ğ‘€¼ğ‘€²ğ‘„, ğ‘€‰ğ‘€ªğ‘„ğ‘€¯ ğ‘€²ğ‘€¦ğ‘†ğ‘€¢ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€‡ğ‘€«ğ‘€ ğ‘€•ğ‘€¡ğ‘€ ğ‘€§ğ‘€­ğ‘€ºğ‘€³ğ‘€­ğ‘€¸ğ‘€«ğ‘€¸â€™ğ‘€¢ğ‘€º.

955\. ğ‘€‡ğ‘€¢ğ‘€º ğ‘€”ğ‘„, ğ‘€ªğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€”ğ‘€¯ğ‘‚, ğ‘€†ğ‘€ğ‘€†ğ‘€­ğ‘„ ğ‘€“ğ‘€¸ğ‘€®ğ‘€¸ğ‘€«ğ‘„ ğ‘€†ğ‘€˜ğ‘€­ğ‘€ºğ‘€¬ğ‘„ ğ‘€«ğ‘‚ ğ‘€²ğ‘€«ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¦ğ‘„ ğ‘€…ğ‘€¢ğ‘†ğ‘€¢ğ‘€¦ğ‘„ ğ‘€…ğ‘€¦ğ‘†ğ‘€¢ğ‘‚ğ‘€¯ğ‘€¸ğ‘€²ğ‘€ºğ‘€ ğ‘€«ğ‘€ ğ‘€²ğ‘€«ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¦ğ‘€ ğ‘€…ğ‘€¢ğ‘†ğ‘€¢ğ‘€¦ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€²ğ‘€«ğ‘€²ğ‘€«ğ‘€ ğ‘€ğ‘€§ğ‘‚ğ‘€²ğ‘€º, ğ‘€‰ğ‘€ğ‘€†ğ‘€­ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¬ ğ‘€˜ ğ‘€«ğ‘€ ğ‘€§ğ‘€½ğ‘€šğ‘€¸ğ‘€¬ ğ‘€§ğ‘€½ğ‘€šğ‘‚ğ‘€²ğ‘€º. ğ‘€¢ğ‘€²ğ‘†ğ‘€² ğ‘€«ğ‘€¬ğ‘†ğ‘€³ğ‘€, ğ‘€ªğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€”ğ‘€¯ğ‘‚, ğ‘€ğ‘€¢ğ‘€¤ğ‘€³ğ‘„ğ‘€²ğ‘€ºâ€”  â€˜ğ‘€¦ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¬ğ‘€ ğ‘€¥ğ‘€«ğ‘†ğ‘€«ğ‘„ ğ‘€¦ğ‘€ºğ‘€©ğ‘†ğ‘€©ğ‘€ºğ‘€¤ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¬ ğ‘€¦ ğ‘€¯ğ‘€ºğ‘€­ğ‘€¸ğ‘€•ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¬ ğ‘€¦ ğ‘€¦ğ‘€ºğ‘€­ğ‘„ğ‘€¥ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¬ ğ‘€¦ ğ‘€‰ğ‘€§ğ‘€²ğ‘€«ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¬ ğ‘€¦ ğ‘€…ğ‘€ªğ‘€ºğ‘€œğ‘†ğ‘€œğ‘€¸ğ‘€¬ ğ‘€¦ ğ‘€²ğ‘€«ğ‘†ğ‘€©ğ‘„ğ‘€¥ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¬ ğ‘€¦ ğ‘€¦ğ‘€ºğ‘€©ğ‘†ğ‘€©ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¦ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¬ ğ‘€²ğ‘€ğ‘€¯ğ‘€¢ğ‘†ğ‘€¢ğ‘€¢ğ‘€º, ğ‘€¬ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¯ğ‘€¤ğ‘‚ğ‘€¯ ğ‘€†ğ‘€“ğ‘€ºğ‘€œğ‘†ğ‘€˜ğ‘€œğ‘†ğ‘€œğ‘€¸ğ‘€¬ğ‘€¢ğ‘€¦ğ‘€½ğ‘€§ğ‘€§ğ‘€¢ğ‘†ğ‘€¢ğ‘€ºğ‘€¬ğ‘€¸â€™ğ‘€¢ğ‘€º. ğ‘€²ğ‘„ ğ‘€”ğ‘„ ğ‘€…ğ‘€³ğ‘€, ğ‘€ªğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€”ğ‘€¯ğ‘‚, ğ‘€¢ğ‘€ ğ‘€¥ğ‘€«ğ‘†ğ‘€«ğ‘€ ğ‘€…ğ‘€¦ğ‘€®ğ‘€—ğ‘†ğ‘€“ğ‘€­ğ‘€ºğ‘€¢ğ‘†ğ‘€¯ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€¢ğ‘€²ğ‘†ğ‘€«ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€¥ğ‘€«ğ‘†ğ‘€«ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€¦ğ‘€ºğ‘€©ğ‘†ğ‘€©ğ‘€ºğ‘€šğ‘†ğ‘€š ğ‘€…ğ‘€§ğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€“ğ‘€«ğ‘€ºğ‘€.

## Uddaka son of RÄma

<Tabs syncKey="paliquote">
<TabItem label="My Translation">
956\. So, bhikkhave, seeking what is wholesome, seeking the unsurpassed state of supreme peace, I approached where Uddaka son of RÄma was. Having approached, I said to Uddaka son of RÄma: â€œI wish, Ävuso, to lead the holy life in this Dhamma and discipline.â€ When I said this, bhikkhave, Uddaka son of RÄma said to me: â€œMay the venerable one stay; such is this Dhamma that a wise person, in no long time, having realized and personally experienced the doctrine of his own teacher, may abide.â€ So, bhikkhave, quickly, in no long time, I learned that Dhamma. So, bhikkhave, merely by lip-service, by mere verbal declaration, I would speak of the doctrine of knowledge and the doctrine of elders, and I would claim, â€˜I know, I see,â€™ and so would others. Then, bhikkhave, this thought occurred to me: â€˜RÄma did not merely declare this Dhamma as something he dwelt in, having realized and personally experienced it by mere faith; surely RÄma dwelt in this Dhamma knowing and seeing it.â€™

957\. Then, bhikkhave, I approached where Uddaka son of RÄma was; having approached, I said to Uddaka son of RÄma: â€˜To what extent, Ävuso, did RÄma declare this Dhamma as something he dwelt in, having realized and personally experienced it?â€™ When I said this, bhikkhave, Uddaka son of RÄma declared the dimension of neither-perception-nor-non-perception. Then, bhikkhave, this thought occurred to me: â€˜It was not only RÄma who had faith; I also have faith; it was not only RÄma who had energy; I also have energy; it was not only RÄma who had mindfulness; I also have mindfulness; it was not only RÄma who had concentration; I also have concentration; it was not only RÄma who had wisdom; I also have wisdom. What if I were to strive for the realization of that Dhamma which RÄma declared he dwelt in, having realized and personally experienced it?â€™ So, bhikkhave, quickly, in no long time, having realized and personally experienced that Dhamma, I dwelt in it.

958\. Then, bhikkhave, I approached where Uddaka son of RÄma was; having approached, I said to Uddaka son of RÄma:

959\. â€˜To what extent, Ävuso, did RÄma declare this Dhamma as something he dwelt in, having realized and personally experienced it?â€™

960\. â€˜To this extent, Ävuso, did RÄma declare this Dhamma as something he dwelt in, having realized and personally experienced it.â€™

961\. â€˜I too, Ävuso, to this extent dwell in this Dhamma, having realized and personally experienced it.â€™

962\. â€˜It is a gain for us, Ävuso, it is a great gain for us, that we see such a venerable one as a co-practitioner. Thus, the Dhamma that RÄma declared, having realized and personally experienced it, that Dhamma you dwell in, having realized and personally experienced it. The Dhamma that you dwell in, having realized and personally experienced it, that Dhamma RÄma declared, having realized and personally experienced it. Thus, the Dhamma that RÄma knew, that Dhamma you know; the Dhamma that you know, that Dhamma RÄma knew. Thus, as RÄma was, so are you; as you are, so was RÄma. Come now, Ävuso, you lead this company.â€™

963\. Thus, bhikkhave, Uddaka son of RÄma, being my co-practitioner, placed me in the position of teacher, and honored me with great honor. Then, bhikkhave, this thought occurred to me: â€˜This Dhamma does not lead to disenchantment, nor to dispassion, nor to cessation, nor to tranquility, nor to direct knowledge, nor to full awakening, nor to Nibbana; it leads only to rebirth in the dimension of neither-perception-nor-non-perception.â€™ So, bhikkhave, not valuing that Dhamma, I became disenchanted with that Dhamma and departed.
</TabItem>

  962\. â€˜ğ‘€®ğ‘€¸ğ‘€ªğ‘€¸ ğ‘€¦ğ‘„, ğ‘€†ğ‘€¯ğ‘€¼ğ‘€²ğ‘„, ğ‘€²ğ‘€¼ğ‘€®ğ‘€¤ğ‘†ğ‘€¥ğ‘€ ğ‘€¦ğ‘„, ğ‘€†ğ‘€¯ğ‘€¼ğ‘€²ğ‘„, ğ‘€¬ğ‘‚ ğ‘€«ğ‘€¬ğ‘€ ğ‘€†ğ‘€¬ğ‘€²ğ‘†ğ‘€«ğ‘€¦ğ‘†ğ‘€¢ğ‘€ ğ‘€¢ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¤ğ‘€ºğ‘€²ğ‘€ ğ‘€²ğ‘€©ğ‘†ğ‘€­ğ‘€³ğ‘†ğ‘€«ğ‘€˜ğ‘€¸ğ‘€­ğ‘€ºğ‘€ ğ‘€§ğ‘€²ğ‘†ğ‘€²ğ‘€¸ğ‘€«. ğ‘€‡ğ‘€¢ğ‘€º ğ‘€¬ğ‘€ ğ‘€¥ğ‘€«ğ‘†ğ‘€«ğ‘€ ğ‘€­ğ‘€¸ğ‘€«ğ‘„ ğ‘€²ğ‘€¬ğ‘€ ğ‘€…ğ‘€ªğ‘€ºğ‘€œğ‘†ğ‘€œğ‘€¸ ğ‘€²ğ‘€˜ğ‘†ğ‘€™ğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘€¢ğ‘†ğ‘€¯ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€‰ğ‘€§ğ‘€²ğ‘€«ğ‘†ğ‘€§ğ‘€šğ‘†ğ‘€š ğ‘€§ğ‘€¯ğ‘‚ğ‘€¤ğ‘‚ğ‘€²ğ‘€º, ğ‘€¢ğ‘€ ğ‘€¢ğ‘†ğ‘€¯ğ‘€ ğ‘€¥ğ‘€«ğ‘†ğ‘€«ğ‘€ ğ‘€²ğ‘€¬ğ‘€ ğ‘€…ğ‘€ªğ‘€ºğ‘€œğ‘†ğ‘€œğ‘€¸ ğ‘€²ğ‘€˜ğ‘†ğ‘€™ğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘€¢ğ‘†ğ‘€¯ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€‰ğ‘€§ğ‘€²ğ‘€«ğ‘†ğ‘€§ğ‘€šğ‘†ğ‘€š ğ‘€¯ğ‘€ºğ‘€³ğ‘€­ğ‘€²ğ‘€º. ğ‘€¬ğ‘€ ğ‘€¢ğ‘†ğ‘€¯ğ‘€ ğ‘€¥ğ‘€«ğ‘†ğ‘€«ğ‘€ ğ‘€²ğ‘€¬ğ‘€ ğ‘€…ğ‘€ªğ‘€ºğ‘€œğ‘†ğ‘€œğ‘€¸ ğ‘€²ğ‘€˜ğ‘†ğ‘€™ğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘€¢ğ‘†ğ‘€¯ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€‰ğ‘€§ğ‘€²ğ‘€«ğ‘†ğ‘€§ğ‘€šğ‘†ğ‘€š ğ‘€¯ğ‘€ºğ‘€³ğ‘€­ğ‘€²ğ‘€º, ğ‘€¢ğ‘€ ğ‘€¥ğ‘€«ğ‘†ğ‘€«ğ‘€ ğ‘€­ğ‘€¸ğ‘€«ğ‘„ ğ‘€²ğ‘€¬ğ‘€ ğ‘€…ğ‘€ªğ‘€ºğ‘€œğ‘†ğ‘€œğ‘€¸ ğ‘€²ğ‘€˜ğ‘†ğ‘€™ğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘€¢ğ‘†ğ‘€¯ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€‰ğ‘€§ğ‘€²ğ‘€«ğ‘†ğ‘€§ğ‘€šğ‘†ğ‘€š ğ‘€§ğ‘€¯ğ‘‚ğ‘€¤ğ‘‚ğ‘€²ğ‘€º. ğ‘€‡ğ‘€¢ğ‘€º ğ‘€¬ğ‘€ ğ‘€¥ğ‘€«ğ‘†ğ‘€«ğ‘€ ğ‘€­ğ‘€¸ğ‘€«ğ‘„ ğ‘€…ğ‘€ªğ‘€ºğ‘€œğ‘†ğ‘€œğ‘€¸ğ‘€²ğ‘€º ğ‘€¢ğ‘€ ğ‘€¢ğ‘†ğ‘€¯ğ‘€ ğ‘€¥ğ‘€«ğ‘†ğ‘€«ğ‘€ ğ‘€šğ‘€¸ğ‘€¦ğ‘€¸ğ‘€²ğ‘€º, ğ‘€¬ğ‘€ ğ‘€¢ğ‘†ğ‘€¯ğ‘€ ğ‘€¥ğ‘€«ğ‘†ğ‘€«ğ‘€ ğ‘€šğ‘€¸ğ‘€¦ğ‘€¸ğ‘€²ğ‘€º, ğ‘€¢ğ‘€ ğ‘€¥ğ‘€«ğ‘†ğ‘€«ğ‘€ ğ‘€­ğ‘€¸ğ‘€«ğ‘„ ğ‘€…ğ‘€ªğ‘€ºğ‘€œğ‘†ğ‘€œğ‘€¸ğ‘€²ğ‘€º. ğ‘€‡ğ‘€¢ğ‘€º ğ‘€¬ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¤ğ‘€ºğ‘€²ğ‘„ ğ‘€­ğ‘€¸ğ‘€«ğ‘„ ğ‘€…ğ‘€³ğ‘„ğ‘€²ğ‘€º ğ‘€¢ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¤ğ‘€ºğ‘€²ğ‘„ ğ‘€¢ğ‘€¼ğ‘€¯ğ‘€, ğ‘€¬ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¤ğ‘€ºğ‘€²ğ‘„ ğ‘€¢ğ‘€¼ğ‘€¯ğ‘€ ğ‘€¢ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¤ğ‘€ºğ‘€²ğ‘„ ğ‘€­ğ‘€¸ğ‘€«ğ‘„ ğ‘€…ğ‘€³ğ‘„ğ‘€²ğ‘€º. ğ‘€ğ‘€³ğ‘€º ğ‘€¤ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¦ğ‘€º, ğ‘€†ğ‘€¯ğ‘€¼ğ‘€²ğ‘„, ğ‘€¢ğ‘€¼ğ‘€¯ğ‘€ ğ‘€‡ğ‘€«ğ‘€ ğ‘€•ğ‘€¡ğ‘€ ğ‘€§ğ‘€­ğ‘€ºğ‘€³ğ‘€­ğ‘€¸â€™ğ‘€¢ğ‘€º.
  
  963\. ğ‘€‡ğ‘€¢ğ‘€º ğ‘€”ğ‘„, ğ‘€ªğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€”ğ‘€¯ğ‘‚, ğ‘€‰ğ‘€¤ğ‘€“ğ‘„ ğ‘€­ğ‘€¸ğ‘€«ğ‘€§ğ‘€¼ğ‘€¢ğ‘†ğ‘€¢ğ‘„ ğ‘€²ğ‘€©ğ‘†ğ‘€­ğ‘€³ğ‘†ğ‘€«ğ‘€˜ğ‘€¸ğ‘€­ğ‘€» ğ‘€«ğ‘‚ ğ‘€²ğ‘€«ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¦ğ‘„ ğ‘€†ğ‘€˜ğ‘€­ğ‘€ºğ‘€¬ğ‘€ğ‘†ğ‘€ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¦ğ‘‚ ğ‘€«ğ‘€ ğ‘€ğ‘€§ğ‘‚ğ‘€²ğ‘€º, ğ‘€‰ğ‘€ğ‘€†ğ‘€­ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¬ ğ‘€˜ ğ‘€«ğ‘€ ğ‘€§ğ‘€½ğ‘€šğ‘€¸ğ‘€¬ ğ‘€§ğ‘€½ğ‘€šğ‘‚ğ‘€²ğ‘€º. ğ‘€¢ğ‘€²ğ‘†ğ‘€² ğ‘€«ğ‘€¬ğ‘†ğ‘€³ğ‘€, ğ‘€ªğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€”ğ‘€¯ğ‘‚, ğ‘€ğ‘€¢ğ‘€¤ğ‘€³ğ‘„ğ‘€²ğ‘€ºâ€”  â€˜ğ‘€¦ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¬ğ‘€ ğ‘€¥ğ‘€«ğ‘†ğ‘€«ğ‘„ ğ‘€¦ğ‘€ºğ‘€©ğ‘†ğ‘€©ğ‘€ºğ‘€¤ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¬ ğ‘€¦ ğ‘€¯ğ‘€ºğ‘€­ğ‘€¸ğ‘€•ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¬ ğ‘€¦ ğ‘€¦ğ‘€ºğ‘€­ğ‘„ğ‘€¥ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¬ ğ‘€¦ ğ‘€‰ğ‘€§ğ‘€²ğ‘€«ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¬ ğ‘€¦ ğ‘€…ğ‘€ªğ‘€ºğ‘€œğ‘†ğ‘€œğ‘€¸ğ‘€¬ ğ‘€¦ ğ‘€²ğ‘€«ğ‘†ğ‘€©ğ‘„ğ‘€¥ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¬ ğ‘€¦ ğ‘€¦ğ‘€ºğ‘€©ğ‘†ğ‘€©ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¦ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¬ ğ‘€²ğ‘€ğ‘€¯ğ‘€¢ğ‘†ğ‘€¢ğ‘€¢ğ‘€º, ğ‘€¬ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¯ğ‘€¤ğ‘‚ğ‘€¯ ğ‘€¦ğ‘‚ğ‘€¯ğ‘€²ğ‘€œğ‘†ğ‘€œğ‘€¸ğ‘€¦ğ‘€¸ğ‘€²ğ‘€œğ‘†ğ‘€œğ‘€¸ğ‘€¬ğ‘€¢ğ‘€¦ğ‘€½ğ‘€§ğ‘€§ğ‘€¢ğ‘†ğ‘€¢ğ‘€ºğ‘€¬ğ‘€¸â€™ğ‘€¢ğ‘€º. ğ‘€²ğ‘„ ğ‘€”ğ‘„ ğ‘€…ğ‘€³ğ‘€, ğ‘€ªğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€”ğ‘€¯ğ‘‚, ğ‘€¢ğ‘€ ğ‘€¥ğ‘€«ğ‘†ğ‘€«ğ‘€ ğ‘€…ğ‘€¦ğ‘€®ğ‘€—ğ‘†ğ‘€“ğ‘€­ğ‘€ºğ‘€¢ğ‘†ğ‘€¯ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€¢ğ‘€²ğ‘†ğ‘€«ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€¥ğ‘€«ğ‘†ğ‘€«ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€¦ğ‘€ºğ‘€©ğ‘†ğ‘€©ğ‘€ºğ‘€šğ‘†ğ‘€š ğ‘€…ğ‘€§ğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€“ğ‘€«ğ‘€ºğ‘€.

## Awakening

<Tabs syncKey="paliquote">
<TabItem label="My Translation">
964\. So, bhikkhave, I, seeking what is wholesome, seeking the unsurpassed state of supreme peace, wandering by stages through Magadha, arrived at UruvelÄ, the army township. There I saw a delightful stretch of land, a beautiful grove, a clear-flowing river with pleasant banks, and a village for alms around it. Then, bhikkhave, this thought occurred to me: â€˜Indeed, delightful is this stretch of land, beautiful is this grove, clear-flowing is this river with pleasant banks, and a village for alms is around it. This is indeed suitable for a clansman intent on striving.â€™ So, bhikkhave, I sat down right there, thinking, â€˜This is enough for striving.â€™

965\. So, bhikkhave, being subject to birth myself, having seen the drawback in what is subject to birth, seeking the unborn, unsurpassed security from bondage, Nibbana, I attained the unborn, unsurpassed security from bondage, Nibbana; being subject to aging myself, having seen the drawback in what is subject to aging, seeking the unaging, unsurpassed security from bondage, Nibbana, I attained the unaging, unsurpassed security from bondage, Nibbana; being subject to sickness myself, having seen the drawback in what is subject to sickness, seeking the unailing, unsurpassed security from bondage, Nibbana, I attained the unailing, unsurpassed security from bondage, Nibbana; being subject to death myself, having seen the drawback in what is subject to death, seeking the undying, unsurpassed security from bondage, Nibbana, I attained the undying, unsurpassed security from bondage, Nibbana; being subject to sorrow myself, having seen the drawback in what is subject to sorrow, seeking the sorrowless, unsurpassed security from bondage, Nibbana, I attained the sorrowless, unsurpassed security from bondage, Nibbana; being subject to defilement myself, having seen the drawback in what is subject to defilement, seeking the undefiled, unsurpassed security from bondage, Nibbana, I attained the undefiled, unsurpassed security from bondage, Nibbana. And knowledge and vision arose in me: â€˜My liberation is unshakeable; this is my last birth; now there is no more renewed existence.â€™
</TabItem>

## Decision to teach

<Tabs syncKey="paliquote">
<TabItem label="My Translation">
966\. So, bhikkhave, this thought occurred to meâ€” â€˜This Dhamma I have realized is profound, hard to see, hard to comprehend, peaceful, refined, beyond the scope of reasoning, subtle, to be experienced by the wise. But this generation delights in attachment, is pleased by attachment, rejoices in attachment. For a generation delighting in attachment, pleased by attachment, rejoicing in attachment, this state is hard to see, namely: specific conditionality, dependent origination. This state, too, is hard to see, namely: the calming of all formations, the relinquishing of all attachments, the destruction of craving, dispassion, cessation, Nibbana. If I were to teach the Dhamma, others would not understand me, that would be weariness for me, that would be trouble for me.â€™ And then, bhikkhave, these unheard-before, wonderful verses occurred to meâ€”

967\. â€˜With difficulty have I realized it, \
enough now with proclaiming it; \
for those consumed by lust and hatred, \
this Dhamma is not easily understood. \
968\. Going against the stream, subtle, \
profound, hard to see, minute; \
those inflamed by lust will not see it, \
shrouded as they are in darkness.â€™

969\. So, bhikkhave, as I reflected thus, my mind inclined to inaction, not to teaching the Dhamma. Then, bhikkhave, BrahmÄ Sahampati, knowing my thought process with his own mind, had this thoughtâ€” â€˜Alas, the world is perishing, alas, the world is being destroyed, inasmuch as the mind of the TathÄgata, the Arahant, the Perfectly Self-Awakened One, inclines to inaction, not to teaching the Dhamma!â€™ Then, bhikkhave, BrahmÄ Sahampatiâ€”just as a strong man might extend his flexed arm or flex his extended armâ€”even so, having vanished from the Brahma-world, appeared before me. Then, bhikkhave, BrahmÄ Sahampati, arranging his upper robe over one shoulder, and having extended his clasped hands towards me, said this to meâ€” â€˜May the BhagavÄ teach the Dhamma, may the Fortunate One teach the Dhamma. There are beings with little dust in their eyes; they are perishing through not hearing the Dhamma. There will be those who understand the Dhamma.â€™ This is what BrahmÄ Sahampati said, bhikkhave. Having said this, he then said furtherâ€”

970\. â€˜In Magadha there arose before, \
an impure Dhamma, conceived by those stained; \
open this gate to the deathless, \
let them hear the Dhamma, realized by the Stainless One. \
971\. Just as one standing on a mountain peak, \
might see the people all around; \
similarly, O wise one, O all-seeing one, \
having ascended the Dhamma-pavilion; \
look down upon the people immersed in sorrow, free from sorrow, \
look upon the people overcome by birth and aging. \
972\. Arise, hero, victor in battle, \
caravan-leader, debtless one, wander in the world; \
may the BhagavÄ teach the Dhamma, \
there will be those who understand it.â€™

973\. Then, bhikkhave, knowing the request of BrahmÄ and out of compassion for beings, I surveyed the world with the Buddha-eye. As I surveyed the world with the Buddha-eye, I saw beings with little dust in their eyes and with much dust in their eyes, with keen faculties and with dull faculties, with good dispositions and with bad dispositions, easy to teach and hard to teach, some who dwelt seeing fear in the blameworthy actions of the next world, and some who did not dwell seeing fear in the blameworthy actions of the next world. Just as in a pond of blue lotuses, or red lotuses, or white lotuses, some blue lotuses, red lotuses, or white lotuses, born in the water, grown in the water, remaining submerged in the water, nourished below the surface; some blue lotuses, red lotuses, or white lotuses, born in the water, grown in the water, standing level with the water; some blue lotuses, red lotuses, or white lotuses, born in the water, grown in the water, rising above the water, unadulterated by the water; even so, bhikkhave, as I surveyed the world with the Buddha-eye, I saw beings with little dust in their eyes and with much dust in their eyes, with keen faculties and with dull faculties, with good dispositions and with bad dispositions, easy to teach and hard to teach, some who dwelt seeing fear in the blameworthy actions of the next world, and some who did not dwell seeing fear in the blameworthy actions of the next world. Then, bhikkhave, I addressed BrahmÄ Sahampati with a verseâ€”

974\. â€˜Open are these doors to the deathless, \
let those with ears release their faith; \
perceiving trouble, I did not teach the Dhamma, \
the subtle Dhamma among humans, O BrahmÄ.â€™

975\. Then, bhikkhave, BrahmÄ Sahampati, thinking, â€˜I have been given an opportunity by the BhagavÄ to teach the Dhamma,â€™ paid homage to me, circumambulated me keeping his right side towards me, and then vanished right there.

969\. ğ‘€‡ğ‘€¢ğ‘€ºğ‘€³ ğ‘€«ğ‘‚, ğ‘€ªğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€”ğ‘€¯ğ‘‚, ğ‘€§ğ‘€ğ‘€ºğ‘€²ğ‘€œğ‘†ğ‘€˜ğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€”ğ‘€¢ğ‘„ ğ‘€…ğ‘€§ğ‘†ğ‘€§ğ‘„ğ‘€²ğ‘†ğ‘€²ğ‘€¼ğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€“ğ‘€¢ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¬ ğ‘€˜ğ‘€ºğ‘€¢ğ‘†ğ‘€¢ğ‘€ ğ‘€¦ğ‘€«ğ‘€¢ğ‘€º, ğ‘€¦ğ‘„ ğ‘€¥ğ‘€«ğ‘†ğ‘€«ğ‘€¤ğ‘‚ğ‘€²ğ‘€¦ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¬. ğ‘€…ğ‘€£ ğ‘€”ğ‘„, ğ‘€ªğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€”ğ‘€¯ğ‘‚, ğ‘€©ğ‘†ğ‘€­ğ‘€³ğ‘†ğ‘€«ğ‘€¼ğ‘€¦ğ‘„ ğ‘€²ğ‘€³ğ‘€«ğ‘†ğ‘€§ğ‘€¢ğ‘€ºğ‘€²ğ‘†ğ‘€² ğ‘€«ğ‘€« ğ‘€˜ğ‘‚ğ‘€¢ğ‘€²ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€˜ğ‘‚ğ‘€¢ğ‘„ğ‘€§ğ‘€­ğ‘€ºğ‘€¯ğ‘€ºğ‘€¢ğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€“ğ‘€«ğ‘€œğ‘†ğ‘€œğ‘€¸ğ‘€¬ ğ‘€ğ‘€¢ğ‘€¤ğ‘€³ğ‘„ğ‘€²ğ‘€ºâ€”  â€˜ğ‘€¦ğ‘€²ğ‘†ğ‘€²ğ‘€¢ğ‘€º ğ‘€¯ğ‘€¢ ğ‘€ªğ‘„ ğ‘€®ğ‘„ğ‘€“ğ‘„, ğ‘€¯ğ‘€ºğ‘€¦ğ‘€²ğ‘†ğ‘€²ğ‘€¢ğ‘€º ğ‘€¯ğ‘€¢ ğ‘€ªğ‘„ ğ‘€®ğ‘„ğ‘€“ğ‘„, ğ‘€¬ğ‘€¢ğ‘†ğ‘€­ ğ‘€³ğ‘€º ğ‘€¦ğ‘€¸ğ‘€« ğ‘€¢ğ‘€£ğ‘€¸ğ‘€•ğ‘€¢ğ‘€²ğ‘†ğ‘€² ğ‘€…ğ‘€­ğ‘€³ğ‘€¢ğ‘„ ğ‘€²ğ‘€«ğ‘†ğ‘€«ğ‘€¸ğ‘€²ğ‘€«ğ‘†ğ‘€©ğ‘€¼ğ‘€¤ğ‘†ğ‘€¥ğ‘€²ğ‘†ğ‘€² ğ‘€…ğ‘€§ğ‘†ğ‘€§ğ‘„ğ‘€²ğ‘†ğ‘€²ğ‘€¼ğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€“ğ‘€¢ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¬ ğ‘€˜ğ‘€ºğ‘€¢ğ‘†ğ‘€¢ğ‘€ ğ‘€¦ğ‘€«ğ‘€¢ğ‘€º, ğ‘€¦ğ‘„ ğ‘€¥ğ‘€«ğ‘†ğ‘€«ğ‘€¤ğ‘‚ğ‘€²ğ‘€¦ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¬ğ‘€¸â€™ğ‘€¢ğ‘€º. ğ‘€…ğ‘€£ ğ‘€”ğ‘„, ğ‘€ªğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€”ğ‘€¯ğ‘‚, ğ‘€©ğ‘†ğ‘€­ğ‘€³ğ‘†ğ‘€«ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€²ğ‘€³ğ‘€«ğ‘†ğ‘€§ğ‘€¢ğ‘€ºâ€”  ğ‘€²ğ‘‚ğ‘€¬ğ‘†ğ‘€¬ğ‘€£ğ‘€¸ğ‘€§ğ‘€º ğ‘€¦ğ‘€¸ğ‘€« ğ‘€©ğ‘€®ğ‘€¯ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€§ğ‘€¼ğ‘€­ğ‘€ºğ‘€²ğ‘„ ğ‘€²ğ‘€«ğ‘€ºğ‘€œğ‘†ğ‘€šğ‘€ºğ‘€¢ğ‘€ ğ‘€¯ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€©ğ‘€¸ğ‘€³ğ‘€ ğ‘€§ğ‘€²ğ‘€¸ğ‘€­ğ‘‚ğ‘€¬ğ‘†ğ‘€¬, ğ‘€§ğ‘€²ğ‘€¸ğ‘€­ğ‘€ºğ‘€¢ğ‘€ ğ‘€¯ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€©ğ‘€¸ğ‘€³ğ‘€ ğ‘€²ğ‘€«ğ‘€ºğ‘€œğ‘†ğ‘€šğ‘‚ğ‘€¬ğ‘†ğ‘€¬; ğ‘€ğ‘€¯ğ‘€«ğ‘‚ğ‘€¯â€”  ğ‘€©ğ‘†ğ‘€­ğ‘€³ğ‘†ğ‘€«ğ‘€®ğ‘„ğ‘€“ğ‘‚ ğ‘€…ğ‘€¦ğ‘†ğ‘€¢ğ‘€­ğ‘€³ğ‘€ºğ‘€¢ğ‘„ ğ‘€«ğ‘€« ğ‘€§ğ‘€¼ğ‘€­ğ‘€¢ğ‘„ ğ‘€§ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¢ğ‘€¼ğ‘€­ğ‘€³ğ‘„ğ‘€²ğ‘€º. ğ‘€…ğ‘€£ ğ‘€”ğ‘„, ğ‘€ªğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€”ğ‘€¯ğ‘‚, ğ‘€©ğ‘†ğ‘€­ğ‘€³ğ‘†ğ‘€«ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€²ğ‘€³ğ‘€«ğ‘†ğ‘€§ğ‘€¢ğ‘€º ğ‘€ğ‘€“ğ‘€ğ‘€²ğ‘€ ğ‘€‰ğ‘€¢ğ‘†ğ‘€¢ğ‘€­ğ‘€¸ğ‘€²ğ‘€—ğ‘†ğ‘€•ğ‘€ ğ‘€“ğ‘€­ğ‘€ºğ‘€¢ğ‘†ğ‘€¯ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€¬ğ‘‚ğ‘€¦ğ‘€¸ğ‘€³ğ‘€ ğ‘€¢ğ‘‚ğ‘€¦ğ‘€œğ‘†ğ‘€šğ‘€®ğ‘€ºğ‘€ ğ‘€§ğ‘€¡ğ‘€¸ğ‘€«ğ‘‚ğ‘€¢ğ‘†ğ‘€¯ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€«ğ‘€ ğ‘€ğ‘€¢ğ‘€¤ğ‘€¯ğ‘„ğ‘€˜â€”  â€˜ğ‘€¤ğ‘‚ğ‘€²ğ‘‚ğ‘€¢ğ‘€¼, ğ‘€ªğ‘€¦ğ‘†ğ‘€¢ğ‘‚, ğ‘€ªğ‘€•ğ‘€¯ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€¥ğ‘€«ğ‘†ğ‘€«ğ‘€, ğ‘€¤ğ‘‚ğ‘€²ğ‘‚ğ‘€¢ğ‘€¼ ğ‘€²ğ‘€¼ğ‘€•ğ‘€¢ğ‘„ ğ‘€¥ğ‘€«ğ‘†ğ‘€«ğ‘€. ğ‘€²ğ‘€¦ğ‘†ğ‘€¢ğ‘€º ğ‘€²ğ‘€¢ğ‘†ğ‘€¢ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€…ğ‘€§ğ‘†ğ‘€§ğ‘€­ğ‘€šğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€”ğ‘€šğ‘€¸ğ‘€¢ğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘€¸, ğ‘€…ğ‘€²ğ‘†ğ‘€²ğ‘€¯ğ‘€¦ğ‘€¢ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€¥ğ‘€«ğ‘†ğ‘€«ğ‘€²ğ‘†ğ‘€² ğ‘€§ğ‘€­ğ‘€ºğ‘€³ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¬ğ‘€¦ğ‘†ğ‘€¢ğ‘€º. ğ‘€ªğ‘€¯ğ‘€ºğ‘€²ğ‘†ğ‘€²ğ‘€¦ğ‘†ğ‘€¢ğ‘€º ğ‘€¥ğ‘€«ğ‘†ğ‘€«ğ‘€²ğ‘†ğ‘€² ğ‘€…ğ‘€œğ‘†ğ‘€œğ‘€¸ğ‘€¢ğ‘€¸ğ‘€­ğ‘„â€™ğ‘€¢ğ‘€º. ğ‘€‡ğ‘€¤ğ‘€«ğ‘€¯ğ‘„ğ‘€˜, ğ‘€ªğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€”ğ‘€¯ğ‘‚, ğ‘€©ğ‘†ğ‘€­ğ‘€³ğ‘†ğ‘€«ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€²ğ‘€³ğ‘€«ğ‘†ğ‘€§ğ‘€¢ğ‘€º. ğ‘€‡ğ‘€¤ğ‘€ ğ‘€¯ğ‘€¢ğ‘†ğ‘€¯ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€…ğ‘€£ğ‘€¸ğ‘€§ğ‘€­ğ‘€ ğ‘€ğ‘€¢ğ‘€¤ğ‘€¯ğ‘„ğ‘€˜â€”

970\. â€˜ğ‘€§ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¢ğ‘€¼ğ‘€­ğ‘€³ğ‘„ğ‘€²ğ‘€º ğ‘€«ğ‘€•ğ‘€¥ğ‘‚ğ‘€²ğ‘€¼ ğ‘€§ğ‘€¼ğ‘€©ğ‘†ğ‘€©ğ‘‚, \

971\. ğ‘€²ğ‘‚ğ‘€®ğ‘‚ ğ‘€¬ğ‘€£ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€§ğ‘€©ğ‘†ğ‘€©ğ‘€¢ğ‘€«ğ‘€¼ğ‘€¤ğ‘†ğ‘€¥ğ‘€¦ğ‘€ºğ‘€ğ‘†ğ‘€ğ‘€ºğ‘€¢ğ‘„, \

974\. â€˜ğ‘€…ğ‘€§ğ‘€¸ğ‘€­ğ‘€¼ğ‘€¢ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€¢ğ‘‚ğ‘€²ğ‘€ ğ‘€…ğ‘€«ğ‘€¢ğ‘€²ğ‘†ğ‘€² ğ‘€¤ğ‘†ğ‘€¯ğ‘€¸ğ‘€­ğ‘€¸, \

976\. ğ‘€¢ğ‘€²ğ‘†ğ‘€² ğ‘€«ğ‘€¬ğ‘†ğ‘€³ğ‘€, ğ‘€ªğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€”ğ‘€¯ğ‘‚, ğ‘€ğ‘€¢ğ‘€¤ğ‘€³ğ‘„ğ‘€²ğ‘€ºâ€”  â€˜ğ‘€“ğ‘€²ğ‘†ğ‘€² ğ‘€¦ğ‘€¼ ğ‘€”ğ‘„ ğ‘€…ğ‘€³ğ‘€ ğ‘€§ğ‘€ğ‘€«ğ‘€ ğ‘€¥ğ‘€«ğ‘†ğ‘€«ğ‘€ ğ‘€¤ğ‘‚ğ‘€²ğ‘‚ğ‘€¬ğ‘†ğ‘€¬ğ‘€; ğ‘€“ğ‘„ ğ‘€‡ğ‘€«ğ‘€ ğ‘€¥ğ‘€«ğ‘†ğ‘€«ğ‘€ ğ‘€”ğ‘€ºğ‘€§ğ‘†ğ‘€§ğ‘€«ğ‘‚ğ‘€¯ ğ‘€†ğ‘€šğ‘€¸ğ‘€¦ğ‘€ºğ‘€²ğ‘†ğ‘€²ğ‘€¢ğ‘€»â€™ğ‘€¢ğ‘€º? ğ‘€¢ğ‘€²ğ‘†ğ‘€² ğ‘€«ğ‘€¬ğ‘†ğ‘€³ğ‘€, ğ‘€ªğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€”ğ‘€¯ğ‘‚, ğ‘€ğ‘€¢ğ‘€¤ğ‘€³ğ‘„ğ‘€²ğ‘€ºâ€”  â€˜ğ‘€…ğ‘€¬ğ‘€ ğ‘€”ğ‘„ ğ‘€†ğ‘€ğ‘€†ğ‘€­ğ‘„ ğ‘€“ğ‘€¸ğ‘€®ğ‘€¸ğ‘€«ğ‘„ ğ‘€§ğ‘€¡ğ‘†ğ‘€Ÿğ‘€ºğ‘€¢ğ‘„ ğ‘€¯ğ‘€ºğ‘€¬ğ‘€¢ğ‘†ğ‘€¢ğ‘„ ğ‘€«ğ‘‚ğ‘€¥ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¯ğ‘€» ğ‘€¤ğ‘€»ğ‘€–ğ‘€­ğ‘€¢ğ‘†ğ‘€¢ğ‘€ ğ‘€…ğ‘€§ğ‘†ğ‘€§ğ‘€­ğ‘€šğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€”ğ‘€šğ‘€¸ğ‘€¢ğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘„. ğ‘€¬ğ‘€¦ğ‘†ğ‘€¦ğ‘€½ğ‘€¦ğ‘€¸ğ‘€³ğ‘€ ğ‘€†ğ‘€ğ‘€†ğ‘€­ğ‘€²ğ‘†ğ‘€² ğ‘€“ğ‘€¸ğ‘€®ğ‘€¸ğ‘€«ğ‘€²ğ‘†ğ‘€² ğ‘€§ğ‘€ğ‘€«ğ‘€ ğ‘€¥ğ‘€«ğ‘†ğ‘€«ğ‘€ ğ‘€¤ğ‘‚ğ‘€²ğ‘‚ğ‘€¬ğ‘†ğ‘€¬ğ‘€. ğ‘€²ğ‘„ ğ‘€‡ğ‘€«ğ‘€ ğ‘€¥ğ‘€«ğ‘†ğ‘€«ğ‘€ ğ‘€”ğ‘€ºğ‘€§ğ‘†ğ‘€§ğ‘€«ğ‘‚ğ‘€¯ ğ‘€†ğ‘€šğ‘€¸ğ‘€¦ğ‘€ºğ‘€²ğ‘†ğ‘€²ğ‘€¢ğ‘€»â€™ğ‘€¢ğ‘€º. ğ‘€…ğ‘€£ ğ‘€”ğ‘„ ğ‘€«ğ‘€, ğ‘€ªğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€”ğ‘€¯ğ‘‚, ğ‘€¤ğ‘‚ğ‘€¯ğ‘€¢ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€‰ğ‘€§ğ‘€²ğ‘€—ğ‘†ğ‘€“ğ‘€«ğ‘€ºğ‘€¢ğ‘†ğ‘€¯ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€ğ‘€¢ğ‘€¤ğ‘€¯ğ‘„ğ‘€˜â€”  â€˜ğ‘€²ğ‘€¢ğ‘†ğ‘€¢ğ‘€¸ğ‘€³ğ‘€“ğ‘€¸ğ‘€®ğ‘€—ğ‘†ğ‘€“ğ‘€¢ğ‘„, ğ‘€ªğ‘€¦ğ‘†ğ‘€¢ğ‘‚, ğ‘€†ğ‘€ğ‘€†ğ‘€­ğ‘„ ğ‘€“ğ‘€¸ğ‘€®ğ‘€¸ğ‘€«ğ‘„â€™ğ‘€¢ğ‘€º. ğ‘€œğ‘€¸ğ‘€¡ğ‘€œğ‘†ğ‘€˜ ğ‘€§ğ‘€¦ ğ‘€«ğ‘‚ ğ‘€¤ğ‘€²ğ‘†ğ‘€²ğ‘€¦ğ‘€ ğ‘€‰ğ‘€¤ğ‘€§ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¤ğ‘€ºâ€”  â€˜ğ‘€²ğ‘€¢ğ‘†ğ‘€¢ğ‘€¸ğ‘€³ğ‘€“ğ‘€¸ğ‘€®ğ‘€—ğ‘†ğ‘€“ğ‘€¢ğ‘„ ğ‘€†ğ‘€ğ‘€†ğ‘€­ğ‘„ ğ‘€“ğ‘€¸ğ‘€®ğ‘€¸ğ‘€«ğ‘„â€™ğ‘€¢ğ‘€º. ğ‘€¢ğ‘€²ğ‘†ğ‘€² ğ‘€«ğ‘€¬ğ‘†ğ‘€³ğ‘€, ğ‘€ªğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€”ğ‘€¯ğ‘‚, ğ‘€ğ‘€¢ğ‘€¤ğ‘€³ğ‘„ğ‘€²ğ‘€ºâ€”  â€˜ğ‘€«ğ‘€³ğ‘€¸ğ‘€šğ‘€¸ğ‘€¦ğ‘€ºğ‘€¬ğ‘„ ğ‘€”ğ‘„ ğ‘€†ğ‘€ğ‘€†ğ‘€­ğ‘„ ğ‘€“ğ‘€¸ğ‘€®ğ‘€¸ğ‘€«ğ‘„. ğ‘€²ğ‘€˜ğ‘‚ ğ‘€³ğ‘€º ğ‘€²ğ‘„ ğ‘€‡ğ‘€«ğ‘€ ğ‘€¥ğ‘€«ğ‘†ğ‘€«ğ‘€ ğ‘€²ğ‘€¼ğ‘€¡ğ‘‚ğ‘€¬ğ‘†ğ‘€¬, ğ‘€”ğ‘€ºğ‘€§ğ‘†ğ‘€§ğ‘€«ğ‘‚ğ‘€¯ ğ‘€†ğ‘€šğ‘€¸ğ‘€¦ğ‘‚ğ‘€¬ğ‘†ğ‘€¬ğ‘€¸â€™ğ‘€¢ğ‘€º.

978\. ğ‘€¢ğ‘€²ğ‘†ğ‘€² ğ‘€«ğ‘€¬ğ‘†ğ‘€³ğ‘€, ğ‘€ªğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€”ğ‘€¯ğ‘‚, ğ‘€ğ‘€¢ğ‘€¤ğ‘€³ğ‘„ğ‘€²ğ‘€ºâ€”  â€˜ğ‘€“ğ‘€²ğ‘†ğ‘€² ğ‘€¦ğ‘€¼ ğ‘€”ğ‘„ ğ‘€…ğ‘€³ğ‘€ ğ‘€§ğ‘€ğ‘€«ğ‘€ ğ‘€¥ğ‘€«ğ‘†ğ‘€«ğ‘€ ğ‘€¤ğ‘‚ğ‘€²ğ‘‚ğ‘€¬ğ‘†ğ‘€¬ğ‘€; ğ‘€“ğ‘„ ğ‘€‡ğ‘€«ğ‘€ ğ‘€¥ğ‘€«ğ‘†ğ‘€«ğ‘€ ğ‘€”ğ‘€ºğ‘€§ğ‘†ğ‘€§ğ‘€«ğ‘‚ğ‘€¯ ğ‘€†ğ‘€šğ‘€¸ğ‘€¦ğ‘€ºğ‘€²ğ‘†ğ‘€²ğ‘€¢ğ‘€»â€™ğ‘€¢ğ‘€º? ğ‘€¢ğ‘€²ğ‘†ğ‘€² ğ‘€«ğ‘€¬ğ‘†ğ‘€³ğ‘€, ğ‘€ªğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€”ğ‘€¯ğ‘‚, ğ‘€ğ‘€¢ğ‘€¤ğ‘€³ğ‘„ğ‘€²ğ‘€ºâ€”  â€˜ğ‘€©ğ‘€³ğ‘€¼ğ‘€“ğ‘€¸ğ‘€­ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€”ğ‘„ ğ‘€«ğ‘‚ ğ‘€§ğ‘€œğ‘†ğ‘€˜ğ‘€¯ğ‘€•ğ‘†ğ‘€•ğ‘€ºğ‘€¬ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€ªğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€”ğ‘€½, ğ‘€¬ğ‘‚ ğ‘€«ğ‘€ ğ‘€§ğ‘€¥ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¦ğ‘€§ğ‘€³ğ‘€ºğ‘€¢ğ‘€¢ğ‘†ğ‘€¢ğ‘€ ğ‘€‰ğ‘€§ğ‘€ğ‘†ğ‘€ğ‘€³ğ‘€ºğ‘€ğ‘€²ğ‘€¼. ğ‘€¬ğ‘€¦ğ‘†ğ‘€¦ğ‘€½ğ‘€¦ğ‘€¸ğ‘€³ğ‘€ ğ‘€§ğ‘€œğ‘†ğ‘€˜ğ‘€¯ğ‘€•ğ‘†ğ‘€•ğ‘€ºğ‘€¬ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¦ğ‘€ ğ‘€ªğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€”ğ‘€½ğ‘€¦ğ‘€ ğ‘€§ğ‘€ğ‘€«ğ‘€ ğ‘€¥ğ‘€«ğ‘†ğ‘€«ğ‘€ ğ‘€¤ğ‘‚ğ‘€²ğ‘‚ğ‘€¬ğ‘†ğ‘€¬ğ‘€¦ğ‘†â€™ğ‘€¢ğ‘€º. ğ‘€¢ğ‘€²ğ‘†ğ‘€² ğ‘€«ğ‘€¬ğ‘†ğ‘€³ğ‘€, ğ‘€ªğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€”ğ‘€¯ğ‘‚, ğ‘€ğ‘€¢ğ‘€¤ğ‘€³ğ‘„ğ‘€²ğ‘€ºâ€”  â€˜ğ‘€“ğ‘€³ğ‘€ ğ‘€¦ğ‘€¼ ğ‘€”ğ‘„ ğ‘€ğ‘€¢ğ‘€­ğ‘€³ğ‘€º ğ‘€§ğ‘€œğ‘†ğ‘€˜ğ‘€¯ğ‘€•ğ‘†ğ‘€•ğ‘€ºğ‘€¬ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€ªğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€”ğ‘€½ ğ‘€¯ğ‘€ºğ‘€³ğ‘€­ğ‘€¦ğ‘†ğ‘€¢ğ‘€»â€™ğ‘€¢ğ‘€º? ğ‘€…ğ‘€¤ğ‘†ğ‘€¤ğ‘€²ğ‘€ ğ‘€”ğ‘„ ğ‘€…ğ‘€³ğ‘€, ğ‘€ªğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€”ğ‘€¯ğ‘‚, ğ‘€¤ğ‘€ºğ‘€©ğ‘†ğ‘€©ğ‘‚ğ‘€¦ ğ‘€˜ğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€”ğ‘€¼ğ‘€¦ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€¯ğ‘€ºğ‘€²ğ‘€¼ğ‘€¤ğ‘†ğ‘€¥ğ‘‚ğ‘€¦ ğ‘€…ğ‘€¢ğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€“ğ‘€¦ğ‘†ğ‘€¢ğ‘€«ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¦ğ‘€¼ğ‘€²ğ‘€“ğ‘‚ğ‘€¦ ğ‘€§ğ‘€œğ‘†ğ‘€˜ğ‘€¯ğ‘€•ğ‘†ğ‘€•ğ‘€ºğ‘€¬ğ‘‚ ğ‘€ªğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€”ğ‘€½ ğ‘€©ğ‘€¸ğ‘€­ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¡ğ‘€²ğ‘€ºğ‘€¬ğ‘€ ğ‘€¯ğ‘€ºğ‘€³ğ‘€­ğ‘€¦ğ‘†ğ‘€¢ğ‘‚ ğ‘€‡ğ‘€²ğ‘€ºğ‘€§ğ‘€¢ğ‘€¦ğ‘‚ ğ‘€«ğ‘€ºğ‘€•ğ‘€¤ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¬ğ‘‚. ğ‘€…ğ‘€£ ğ‘€”ğ‘†ğ‘€¯ğ‘€¸ğ‘€³ğ‘€, ğ‘€ªğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€”ğ‘€¯ğ‘‚, ğ‘€‰ğ‘€­ğ‘€¼ğ‘€¯ğ‘‚ğ‘€®ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¬ğ‘€ ğ‘€¬ğ‘€£ğ‘€¸ğ‘€ªğ‘€ºğ‘€­ğ‘€¦ğ‘†ğ‘€¢ğ‘€ ğ‘€¯ğ‘€ºğ‘€³ğ‘€­ğ‘€ºğ‘€¢ğ‘†ğ‘€¯ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€¬ğ‘‚ğ‘€¦ ğ‘€©ğ‘€¸ğ‘€­ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¡ğ‘€²ğ‘€» ğ‘€¢ğ‘‚ğ‘€¦ ğ‘€˜ğ‘€¸ğ‘€­ğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘€ ğ‘€§ğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€“ğ‘€«ğ‘€ºğ‘€.

## Upaka the Ä€jÄ«vaka

<Tabs syncKey="paliquote">
<TabItem label="My Translation">
979\. Then, bhikkhave, Upaka the Ä€jÄ«vaka saw me proceeding on the highway between Gaya and Bodhi. Having seen me, he said this: â€˜Your faculties, Ävuso, are clear; your skin is pure and bright. For whom, Ävuso, have you gone forth? Who is your teacher? Whose Dhamma do you approve of?â€™ When this was said, I, bhikkhave, addressed Upaka the Ä€jÄ«vaka with verses:

980\. â€˜I am the all-subduing, the all-knowing,
Untainted by all things;
Having abandoned all, released through the destruction of craving,
Having directly known by myself, whom should I point to as my teacher?
981\. I have no teacher,
a peer for me does not exist;
In the world with its gods,
there is no one equal to me.
982\. For I am an Arahant in the world,
I am the supreme teacher;
I alone am the perfectly self-awakened one,
cooled am I, extinguished.
983\. To set in motion the wheel of Dhamma,
I go to the city of KÄsi (BÄrÄá¹‡asÄ«);
In this world that has become blind,
I shall beat the drum of the Deathless.â€™

984\. â€˜As you affirm, Ävuso, you are worthy to be an infinite victor!â€™

985\. â€˜Indeed, such as I are conquerors,
those who have reached the destruction of defilements;
Evil things have been conquered by me,
therefore, Upaka, I am a conqueror.â€™

986\. When this was said, bhikkhave, Upaka the Ä€jÄ«vaka, saying â€˜Perhaps, Ävusoâ€™, shook his head, took a by-path, and departed.

979\. ğ‘€…ğ‘€¤ğ‘†ğ‘€¤ğ‘€²ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€”ğ‘„ ğ‘€«ğ‘€, ğ‘€ªğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€”ğ‘€¯ğ‘‚, ğ‘€‰ğ‘€§ğ‘€“ğ‘„ ğ‘€†ğ‘€šğ‘€»ğ‘€¯ğ‘€“ğ‘„ ğ‘€…ğ‘€¦ğ‘†ğ‘€¢ğ‘€­ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€˜ ğ‘€•ğ‘€¬ğ‘€ ğ‘€…ğ‘€¦ğ‘†ğ‘€¢ğ‘€­ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€˜ ğ‘€©ğ‘„ğ‘€¥ğ‘€ºğ‘€ ğ‘€…ğ‘€¤ğ‘†ğ‘€¥ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¦ğ‘€«ğ‘€•ğ‘†ğ‘€•ğ‘€§ğ‘†ğ‘€§ğ‘€ğ‘€ºğ‘€§ğ‘€¦ğ‘†ğ‘€¦ğ‘€. ğ‘€¤ğ‘€ºğ‘€²ğ‘†ğ‘€¯ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¦ ğ‘€«ğ‘€ ğ‘€ğ‘€¢ğ‘€¤ğ‘€¯ğ‘„ğ‘€˜â€”  â€˜ğ‘€¯ğ‘€ºğ‘€§ğ‘†ğ‘€§ğ‘€²ğ‘€¦ğ‘†ğ‘€¦ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¦ğ‘€º ğ‘€”ğ‘„ ğ‘€¢ğ‘‚, ğ‘€†ğ‘€¯ğ‘€¼ğ‘€²ğ‘„, ğ‘€‡ğ‘€¦ğ‘†ğ‘€¤ğ‘†ğ‘€­ğ‘€ºğ‘€¬ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¦ğ‘€º, ğ‘€§ğ‘€­ğ‘€ºğ‘€²ğ‘€¼ğ‘€¤ğ‘†ğ‘€¥ğ‘„ ğ‘€™ğ‘€¯ğ‘€ºğ‘€¯ğ‘€¡ğ‘†ğ‘€¡ğ‘„ ğ‘€§ğ‘€­ğ‘€ºğ‘€¬ğ‘„ğ‘€¤ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¢ğ‘„. ğ‘€“ğ‘€ğ‘€²ğ‘€º ğ‘€¢ğ‘†ğ‘€¯ğ‘€, ğ‘€†ğ‘€¯ğ‘€¼ğ‘€²ğ‘„, ğ‘€‰ğ‘€¤ğ‘†ğ‘€¤ğ‘€ºğ‘€²ğ‘†ğ‘€² ğ‘€§ğ‘€©ğ‘†ğ‘€©ğ‘€šğ‘€ºğ‘€¢ğ‘„, ğ‘€“ğ‘„ ğ‘€¯ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€¢ğ‘‚ ğ‘€²ğ‘€¢ğ‘†ğ‘€£ğ‘€¸, ğ‘€“ğ‘€²ğ‘†ğ‘€² ğ‘€¯ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€¢ğ‘†ğ‘€¯ğ‘€ ğ‘€¥ğ‘€«ğ‘†ğ‘€«ğ‘€ ğ‘€­ğ‘„ğ‘€˜ğ‘‚ğ‘€²ğ‘€»â€™ğ‘€¢ğ‘€º? ğ‘€ğ‘€¯ğ‘€ ğ‘€¯ğ‘€¼ğ‘€¢ğ‘†ğ‘€¢ğ‘‚, ğ‘€…ğ‘€³ğ‘€, ğ‘€ªğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€”ğ‘€¯ğ‘‚, ğ‘€‰ğ‘€§ğ‘€“ğ‘€ ğ‘€†ğ‘€šğ‘€»ğ‘€¯ğ‘€“ğ‘€ ğ‘€•ğ‘€¸ğ‘€£ğ‘€¸ğ‘€³ğ‘€º ğ‘€…ğ‘€šğ‘†ğ‘€›ğ‘€ªğ‘€¸ğ‘€²ğ‘€ºğ‘€â€”

980\. â€˜ğ‘€²ğ‘€©ğ‘†ğ‘€©ğ‘€¸ğ‘€ªğ‘€ºğ‘€ªğ‘€½ ğ‘€²ğ‘€©ğ‘†ğ‘€©ğ‘€¯ğ‘€ºğ‘€¤ğ‘€½ğ‘€³ğ‘€«ğ‘€²ğ‘†ğ‘€«ğ‘€º,

## The Five Companions

<Tabs syncKey="paliquote">
<TabItem label="My Translation">
987\. Then, bhikkhave, I, wandering by stages, arrived at BÄrÄá¹‡asÄ«, Isipatana, in the Deer Park, where the group of five bhikkhave were. And, bhikkhave, the group of five bhikkhave saw me coming from afar. Having seen me, they agreed among themselves: â€˜Ävuso, this ascetic Gotama is coming, who has become luxurious, given up striving, and reverted to luxury. He should neither be greeted nor risen to meet; nor should his bowl and robe be received. Nevertheless, a seat should be prepared, (so that) if he wishes, he may sit down.â€™ However, bhikkhave, as I approached, the group of five bhikkhave were unable to keep their own agreement. Some came forward to meet me, received my bowl and robe, some prepared a seat, some set out water for my feet. But they addressed me by name and with the address â€˜Ävusoâ€™.

988\. When this was said, I, bhikkhave, said to the group of five bhikkhave: â€˜bhikkhave, do not address the TathÄgata by name or with the address â€˜Ävusoâ€™. The TathÄgata, bhikkhave, is an Arahant, a Perfectly Self-Awakened One. Lend your ear, bhikkhave! The Deathless has been attained. I instruct, I teach the Dhamma. Acting in accordance with the instruction, before longâ€”for the sake of which noble sons rightly go forth from home to homelessness, that unsurpassed culmination of the spiritual lifeâ€”you will realize it for yourselves with direct knowledge in this very life, and dwell in it.â€™ When this was said, bhikkhave, the group of five bhikkhave said to me: â€˜Even with that posture, that practice, that difficult asceticism, Ävuso Gotama, you did not attain any superhuman state, any noble knowledge and vision special distinction. How then will you now, having become luxurious, given up striving, and reverted to luxury, attain any superhuman state, any noble knowledge and vision special distinction?â€™ When this was said, I, bhikkhave, said to the group of five bhikkhave: â€˜bhikkhave, the TathÄgata is not luxurious, nor has he given up striving, nor has he reverted to luxury. The TathÄgata, bhikkhave, is an Arahant, a Perfectly Self-Awakened One. Lend your ear, bhikkhave! The Deathless has been attained. I instruct, I teach the Dhamma. Acting in accordance with the instruction, before longâ€”for the sake of which noble sons rightly go forth from home to homelessness, that unsurpassed culmination of the spiritual lifeâ€”you will realize it for yourselves with direct knowledge in this very life, and dwell in it.â€™

989\. A second time, bhikkhave, the group of five bhikkhave said to me: â€˜Even with that posture, that practice, that difficult asceticism, Ävuso Gotama, you did not attain any superhuman state, any noble knowledge and vision special distinction. How then will you now, having become luxurious, given up striving, and reverted to luxury, attain any superhuman state, any noble knowledge and vision special distinction?â€™ A second time, I, bhikkhave, said to the group of five bhikkhave: â€˜bhikkhave, the TathÄgata is not luxurious, nor has he given up striving, nor has he reverted to luxury. The TathÄgata, bhikkhave, is an Arahant, a Perfectly Self-Awakened One. Lend your ear, bhikkhave! The Deathless has been attained. I instruct, I teach the Dhamma. Acting in accordance with the instruction, before longâ€”for the sake of which noble sons rightly go forth from home to homelessness, that unsurpassed culmination of the spiritual lifeâ€”you will realize it for yourselves with direct knowledge in this very life, and dwell in it.â€™

990\. A third time, bhikkhave, the group of five bhikkhave said to me: â€˜Even with that posture, that practice, that difficult asceticism, Ävuso Gotama, you did not attain any superhuman state, any noble knowledge and vision special distinction. How then will you now, having become luxurious, given up striving, and reverted to luxury, attain any superhuman state, any noble knowledge and vision special distinction?â€™

991\. When this was said, I, bhikkhave, said to the group of five bhikkhave: â€˜Do you recall, bhikkhave, ever hearing me speak in such a way before this?â€™ â€˜No, Bhante.â€™ â€˜The TathÄgata, bhikkhave, is an Arahant, a Perfectly Self-Awakened One. Lend your ear, bhikkhave! The Deathless has been attained. I instruct, I teach the Dhamma. Acting in accordance with the instruction, before longâ€”for the sake of which noble sons rightly go forth from home to homelessness, that unsurpassed culmination of the spiritual lifeâ€”you will realize it for yourselves with direct knowledge in this very life, and dwell in it.â€™

992\. Then, bhikkhave, I was able to convince the group of five bhikkhave. It so happened, bhikkhave, that I would instruct two bhikkhave, while three bhikkhave went for alms. Whatever the three bhikkhave brought back from going for alms, with that we six sustained ourselves. It so happened, bhikkhave, that I would instruct three bhikkhave, while two bhikkhave went for alms. Whatever the two bhikkhave brought back from going for alms, with that we six sustained ourselves.

993\. Then, bhikkhave, as the group of five bhikkhave were thus instructed and admonished by me, being subject to birth themselves, having seen the drawback in what is subject to birth, seeking the unborn, unsurpassed security from bondage, Nibbana, they attained the unborn, unsurpassed security from bondage, Nibbana. Being subject to aging themselves, having seen the drawback in what is subject to aging, seeking the unaging, unsurpassed security from bondage, Nibbana, they attained the unaging, unsurpassed security from bondage, Nibbana. Being subject to sickness themselves, having seen the drawback in what is subject to sickness, seeking the unailing, unsurpassed security from bondage, Nibbana, they attained the unailing, unsurpassed security from bondage, Nibbana. Being subject to death themselves, having seen the drawback in what is subject to death, seeking the undying, unsurpassed security from bondage, Nibbana, they attained the undying, unsurpassed security from bondage, Nibbana. Being subject to sorrow themselves, having seen the drawback in what is subject to sorrow, seeking the sorrowless, unsurpassed security from bondage, Nibbana, they attained the sorrowless, unsurpassed security from bondage, Nibbana. Being subject to defilement themselves, having seen the drawback in what is subject to defilement, seeking the undefiled, unsurpassed security from bondage, Nibbana, they attained the undefiled, unsurpassed security from bondage, Nibbana. And knowledge and vision arose in them: â€˜Our liberation is unshakeable; this is our last birth; now there is no more renewed existence.â€™

987\. ğ‘€…ğ‘€£ ğ‘€”ğ‘†ğ‘€¯ğ‘€¸ğ‘€³ğ‘€, ğ‘€ªğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€”ğ‘€¯ğ‘‚, ğ‘€…ğ‘€¦ğ‘€¼ğ‘€§ğ‘€¼ğ‘€©ğ‘†ğ‘€©ğ‘‚ğ‘€¦ ğ‘€˜ğ‘€¸ğ‘€­ğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘€ ğ‘€˜ğ‘€­ğ‘€«ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¦ğ‘„ ğ‘€¬ğ‘‚ğ‘€¦ ğ‘€©ğ‘€¸ğ‘€­ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¡ğ‘€²ğ‘€» ğ‘€‡ğ‘€²ğ‘€ºğ‘€§ğ‘€¢ğ‘€¦ğ‘€ ğ‘€«ğ‘€ºğ‘€•ğ‘€¤ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¬ğ‘„ ğ‘€¬ğ‘‚ğ‘€¦ ğ‘€§ğ‘€œğ‘†ğ‘€˜ğ‘€¯ğ‘€•ğ‘†ğ‘€•ğ‘€ºğ‘€¬ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€ªğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€”ğ‘€½ ğ‘€¢ğ‘‚ğ‘€¦ğ‘€¼ğ‘€§ğ‘€²ğ‘€—ğ‘†ğ‘€“ğ‘€«ğ‘€ºğ‘€. ğ‘€…ğ‘€¤ğ‘†ğ‘€¤ğ‘€²ğ‘€ğ‘€²ğ‘€¼ ğ‘€”ğ‘„ ğ‘€«ğ‘€, ğ‘€ªğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€”ğ‘€¯ğ‘‚, ğ‘€§ğ‘€œğ‘†ğ‘€˜ğ‘€¯ğ‘€•ğ‘†ğ‘€•ğ‘€ºğ‘€¬ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€ªğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€”ğ‘€½ ğ‘€¤ğ‘€½ğ‘€­ğ‘€¢ğ‘„ ğ‘€†ğ‘€•ğ‘€˜ğ‘†ğ‘€™ğ‘€¦ğ‘†ğ‘€¢ğ‘€. ğ‘€¤ğ‘€ºğ‘€²ğ‘†ğ‘€¯ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¦ ğ‘€…ğ‘€œğ‘†ğ‘€œğ‘€«ğ‘€œğ‘†ğ‘€œğ‘€ ğ‘€²ğ‘€¡ğ‘†ğ‘€ğ‘€§ğ‘‚ğ‘€²ğ‘€¼ğ‘€â€”  â€˜ğ‘€…ğ‘€¬ğ‘€ ğ‘€”ğ‘„, ğ‘€†ğ‘€¯ğ‘€¼ğ‘€²ğ‘„, ğ‘€²ğ‘€«ğ‘€¡ğ‘„ ğ‘€•ğ‘„ğ‘€¢ğ‘€«ğ‘„ ğ‘€†ğ‘€•ğ‘€˜ğ‘†ğ‘€™ğ‘€¢ğ‘€º ğ‘€©ğ‘€¸ğ‘€³ğ‘€¼ğ‘€®ğ‘†ğ‘€®ğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘„ ğ‘€§ğ‘€¥ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¦ğ‘€¯ğ‘€ºğ‘€©ğ‘†ğ‘€ªğ‘€¦ğ‘†ğ‘€¢ğ‘„ ğ‘€†ğ‘€¯ğ‘€¢ğ‘†ğ‘€¢ğ‘„ ğ‘€©ğ‘€¸ğ‘€³ğ‘€¼ğ‘€®ğ‘†ğ‘€®ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¬. ğ‘€²ğ‘„ ğ‘€¦ğ‘‚ğ‘€¯ ğ‘€…ğ‘€ªğ‘€ºğ‘€¯ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¤ğ‘‚ğ‘€¢ğ‘€©ğ‘†ğ‘€©ğ‘„, ğ‘€¦ ğ‘€§ğ‘€˜ğ‘†ğ‘€˜ğ‘€¼ğ‘€ğ‘†ğ‘€ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¢ğ‘€©ğ‘†ğ‘€©ğ‘„; ğ‘€¦ğ‘€¸ğ‘€²ğ‘†ğ‘€² ğ‘€§ğ‘€¢ğ‘†ğ‘€¢ğ‘€˜ğ‘€»ğ‘€¯ğ‘€­ğ‘€ ğ‘€§ğ‘€ğ‘€ºğ‘€•ğ‘†ğ‘€•ğ‘€³ğ‘‚ğ‘€¢ğ‘€©ğ‘†ğ‘€©ğ‘€. ğ‘€…ğ‘€§ğ‘€º ğ‘€˜ ğ‘€”ğ‘„ ğ‘€†ğ‘€²ğ‘€¦ğ‘€ ğ‘€ğ‘€§ğ‘‚ğ‘€¢ğ‘€©ğ‘†ğ‘€©ğ‘€, ğ‘€²ğ‘€˜ğ‘‚ ğ‘€†ğ‘€“ğ‘€—ğ‘†ğ‘€”ğ‘€ºğ‘€²ğ‘†ğ‘€²ğ‘€¢ğ‘€º ğ‘€¦ğ‘€ºğ‘€²ğ‘€»ğ‘€¤ğ‘€ºğ‘€²ğ‘†ğ‘€²ğ‘€¢ğ‘€»â€™ğ‘€¢ğ‘€º. ğ‘€¬ğ‘€£ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€¬ğ‘€£ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€”ğ‘„ ğ‘€…ğ‘€³ğ‘€, ğ‘€ªğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€”ğ‘€¯ğ‘‚, ğ‘€‰ğ‘€§ğ‘€²ğ‘€—ğ‘†ğ‘€“ğ‘€«ğ‘€ºğ‘€ ğ‘€¢ğ‘€£ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€¢ğ‘€£ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€§ğ‘€œğ‘†ğ‘€˜ğ‘€¯ğ‘€•ğ‘†ğ‘€•ğ‘€ºğ‘€¬ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€ªğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€”ğ‘€½ ğ‘€¦ğ‘€¸ğ‘€²ğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€”ğ‘€ºğ‘€ğ‘€²ğ‘€¼ ğ‘€²ğ‘€“ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¬ ğ‘€“ğ‘€¢ğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¬ ğ‘€²ğ‘€¡ğ‘†ğ‘€ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¢ğ‘€¼ğ‘€. ğ‘€…ğ‘€§ğ‘†ğ‘€§ğ‘‚ğ‘€“ğ‘€˜ğ‘†ğ‘€˜ğ‘‚ ğ‘€«ğ‘€ ğ‘€§ğ‘€˜ğ‘†ğ‘€˜ğ‘€¼ğ‘€•ğ‘†ğ‘€•ğ‘€¦ğ‘†ğ‘€¢ğ‘†ğ‘€¯ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€§ğ‘€¢ğ‘†ğ‘€¢ğ‘€˜ğ‘€»ğ‘€¯ğ‘€­ğ‘€ ğ‘€§ğ‘€ğ‘€ºğ‘€•ğ‘†ğ‘€•ğ‘€³ğ‘‚ğ‘€²ğ‘€¼ğ‘€, ğ‘€…ğ‘€§ğ‘†ğ‘€§ğ‘‚ğ‘€“ğ‘€˜ğ‘†ğ‘€˜ğ‘‚ ğ‘€†ğ‘€²ğ‘€¦ğ‘€ ğ‘€§ğ‘€œğ‘†ğ‘€œğ‘€§ğ‘‚ğ‘€²ğ‘€¼ğ‘€, ğ‘€…ğ‘€§ğ‘†ğ‘€§ğ‘‚ğ‘€“ğ‘€˜ğ‘†ğ‘€˜ğ‘‚ ğ‘€§ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¤ğ‘„ğ‘€¤ğ‘€“ğ‘€ ğ‘€‰ğ‘€§ğ‘€ğ‘†ğ‘€ğ‘€§ğ‘‚ğ‘€²ğ‘€¼ğ‘€. ğ‘€…ğ‘€§ğ‘€º ğ‘€˜ ğ‘€”ğ‘„ ğ‘€«ğ‘€ ğ‘€¦ğ‘€¸ğ‘€«ğ‘‚ğ‘€¦ ğ‘€˜ ğ‘€†ğ‘€¯ğ‘€¼ğ‘€²ğ‘„ğ‘€¯ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¤ğ‘‚ğ‘€¦ ğ‘€˜ ğ‘€²ğ‘€«ğ‘€¼ğ‘€¤ğ‘€¸ğ‘€˜ğ‘€­ğ‘€¦ğ‘†ğ‘€¢ğ‘€º.

988\. ğ‘€ğ‘€¯ğ‘€ ğ‘€¯ğ‘€¼ğ‘€¢ğ‘†ğ‘€¢ğ‘‚, ğ‘€…ğ‘€³ğ‘€, ğ‘€ªğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€”ğ‘€¯ğ‘‚, ğ‘€§ğ‘€œğ‘†ğ‘€˜ğ‘€¯ğ‘€•ğ‘†ğ‘€•ğ‘€ºğ‘€¬ğ‘‚ ğ‘€ªğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€”ğ‘€½ ğ‘€ğ‘€¢ğ‘€¤ğ‘€¯ğ‘„ğ‘€˜ğ‘€â€”  â€˜ğ‘€«ğ‘€¸, ğ‘€ªğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€”ğ‘€¯ğ‘‚, ğ‘€¢ğ‘€£ğ‘€¸ğ‘€•ğ‘€¢ğ‘€ ğ‘€¦ğ‘€¸ğ‘€«ğ‘‚ğ‘€¦ ğ‘€˜ ğ‘€†ğ‘€¯ğ‘€¼ğ‘€²ğ‘„ğ‘€¯ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¤ğ‘‚ğ‘€¦ ğ‘€˜ ğ‘€²ğ‘€«ğ‘€¼ğ‘€¤ğ‘€¸ğ‘€˜ğ‘€­ğ‘€£. ğ‘€…ğ‘€­ğ‘€³ğ‘€, ğ‘€ªğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€”ğ‘€¯ğ‘‚, ğ‘€¢ğ‘€£ğ‘€¸ğ‘€•ğ‘€¢ğ‘„ ğ‘€²ğ‘€«ğ‘†ğ‘€«ğ‘€¸ğ‘€²ğ‘€«ğ‘†ğ‘€©ğ‘€¼ğ‘€¤ğ‘†ğ‘€¥ğ‘„. ğ‘€‘ğ‘€¤ğ‘€³ğ‘€£, ğ‘€ªğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€”ğ‘€¯ğ‘‚, ğ‘€²ğ‘„ğ‘€¢ğ‘€, ğ‘€…ğ‘€«ğ‘€¢ğ‘€«ğ‘€¥ğ‘€ºğ‘€•ğ‘€¢ğ‘€, ğ‘€…ğ‘€³ğ‘€«ğ‘€¦ğ‘€¼ğ‘€²ğ‘€¸ğ‘€²ğ‘€¸ğ‘€«ğ‘€º, ğ‘€…ğ‘€³ğ‘€ ğ‘€¥ğ‘€«ğ‘†ğ‘€«ğ‘€ ğ‘€¤ğ‘‚ğ‘€²ğ‘‚ğ‘€«ğ‘€º. ğ‘€¬ğ‘€£ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¦ğ‘€¼ğ‘€²ğ‘€ºğ‘€ğ‘†ğ‘€ğ‘€ ğ‘€¢ğ‘€£ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€§ğ‘€ğ‘€ºğ‘€§ğ‘€šğ‘†ğ‘€šğ‘€«ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¦ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€¦ğ‘€˜ğ‘€ºğ‘€­ğ‘€²ğ‘†ğ‘€²ğ‘‚ğ‘€¯â€”  ğ‘€¬ğ‘€²ğ‘†ğ‘€²ğ‘€¢ğ‘†ğ‘€£ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¬ ğ‘€“ğ‘€¼ğ‘€®ğ‘€§ğ‘€¼ğ‘€¢ğ‘†ğ‘€¢ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€²ğ‘€«ğ‘†ğ‘€«ğ‘€¤ğ‘‚ğ‘€¯ ğ‘€…ğ‘€•ğ‘€¸ğ‘€­ğ‘€²ğ‘†ğ‘€«ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€…ğ‘€¦ğ‘€•ğ‘€¸ğ‘€­ğ‘€ºğ‘€¬ğ‘€ ğ‘€§ğ‘€©ğ‘†ğ‘€©ğ‘€šğ‘€¦ğ‘†ğ‘€¢ğ‘€º, ğ‘€¢ğ‘€¤ğ‘€¦ğ‘€¼ğ‘€¢ğ‘†ğ‘€¢ğ‘€­ğ‘€â€”  ğ‘€©ğ‘†ğ‘€­ğ‘€³ğ‘†ğ‘€«ğ‘€˜ğ‘€­ğ‘€ºğ‘€¬ğ‘€§ğ‘€­ğ‘€ºğ‘€¬ğ‘„ğ‘€²ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¦ğ‘€ ğ‘€¤ğ‘€ºğ‘€ğ‘†ğ‘€ğ‘‚ğ‘€¯ ğ‘€¥ğ‘€«ğ‘†ğ‘€«ğ‘‚ ğ‘€²ğ‘€¬ğ‘€ ğ‘€…ğ‘€ªğ‘€ºğ‘€œğ‘†ğ‘€œğ‘€¸ ğ‘€²ğ‘€˜ğ‘†ğ‘€™ğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘€¢ğ‘†ğ‘€¯ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€‰ğ‘€§ğ‘€²ğ‘€«ğ‘†ğ‘€§ğ‘€šğ‘†ğ‘€š ğ‘€¯ğ‘€ºğ‘€³ğ‘€­ğ‘€ºğ‘€²ğ‘†ğ‘€²ğ‘€£ğ‘€¸â€™ğ‘€¢ğ‘€º. ğ‘€ğ‘€¯ğ‘€ ğ‘€¯ğ‘€¼ğ‘€¢ğ‘†ğ‘€¢ğ‘‚, ğ‘€ªğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€”ğ‘€¯ğ‘‚, ğ‘€§ğ‘€œğ‘†ğ‘€˜ğ‘€¯ğ‘€•ğ‘†ğ‘€•ğ‘€ºğ‘€¬ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€ªğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€”ğ‘€½ ğ‘€«ğ‘€ ğ‘€ğ‘€¢ğ‘€¤ğ‘€¯ğ‘„ğ‘€˜ğ‘€¼ğ‘€â€”  â€˜ğ‘€¢ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¬ğ‘€§ğ‘€º ğ‘€”ğ‘„ ğ‘€¢ğ‘†ğ‘€¯ğ‘€, ğ‘€†ğ‘€¯ğ‘€¼ğ‘€²ğ‘„ ğ‘€•ğ‘„ğ‘€¢ğ‘€«, ğ‘€‡ğ‘€­ğ‘€ºğ‘€¬ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¬ ğ‘€¢ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¬ ğ‘€§ğ‘€ğ‘€ºğ‘€§ğ‘€¤ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¬ ğ‘€¢ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¬ ğ‘€¤ğ‘€¼ğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€“ğ‘€­ğ‘€“ğ‘€¸ğ‘€­ğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¬ ğ‘€¦ğ‘€¸ğ‘€šğ‘†ğ‘€›ğ‘€•ğ‘€«ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€‰ğ‘€¢ğ‘†ğ‘€¢ğ‘€­ğ‘€º ğ‘€«ğ‘€¦ğ‘€¼ğ‘€²ğ‘†ğ‘€²ğ‘€¥ğ‘€«ğ‘†ğ‘€«ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€…ğ‘€®ğ‘€«ğ‘€­ğ‘€ºğ‘€¬ğ‘€œğ‘€¸ğ‘€¡ğ‘€¤ğ‘€²ğ‘†ğ‘€²ğ‘€¦ğ‘€¯ğ‘€ºğ‘€²ğ‘‚ğ‘€²ğ‘€, ğ‘€“ğ‘€ºğ‘€ ğ‘€§ğ‘€¦ ğ‘€¢ğ‘†ğ‘€¯ğ‘€ ğ‘€ğ‘€¢ğ‘€­ğ‘€³ğ‘€º ğ‘€©ğ‘€¸ğ‘€³ğ‘€¼ğ‘€®ğ‘†ğ‘€®ğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘„ ğ‘€§ğ‘€¥ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¦ğ‘€¯ğ‘€ºğ‘€©ğ‘†ğ‘€ªğ‘€¦ğ‘†ğ‘€¢ğ‘„ ğ‘€†ğ‘€¯ğ‘€¢ğ‘†ğ‘€¢ğ‘„ ğ‘€©ğ‘€¸ğ‘€³ğ‘€¼ğ‘€®ğ‘†ğ‘€®ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¬ ğ‘€…ğ‘€¥ğ‘€ºğ‘€•ğ‘€«ğ‘€ºğ‘€²ğ‘†ğ‘€²ğ‘€²ğ‘€º ğ‘€‰ğ‘€¢ğ‘†ğ‘€¢ğ‘€­ğ‘€º ğ‘€«ğ‘€¦ğ‘€¼ğ‘€²ğ‘†ğ‘€²ğ‘€¥ğ‘€«ğ‘†ğ‘€«ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€…ğ‘€®ğ‘€«ğ‘€­ğ‘€ºğ‘€¬ğ‘€œğ‘€¸ğ‘€¡ğ‘€¤ğ‘€²ğ‘†ğ‘€²ğ‘€¦ğ‘€¯ğ‘€ºğ‘€²ğ‘‚ğ‘€²ğ‘€¦ğ‘†â€™ğ‘€¢ğ‘€º? ğ‘€ğ‘€¯ğ‘€ ğ‘€¯ğ‘€¼ğ‘€¢ğ‘†ğ‘€¢ğ‘‚, ğ‘€…ğ‘€³ğ‘€, ğ‘€ªğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€”ğ‘€¯ğ‘‚, ğ‘€§ğ‘€œğ‘†ğ‘€˜ğ‘€¯ğ‘€•ğ‘†ğ‘€•ğ‘€ºğ‘€¬ğ‘‚ ğ‘€ªğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€”ğ‘€½ ğ‘€ğ‘€¢ğ‘€¤ğ‘€¯ğ‘„ğ‘€˜ğ‘€â€”  â€˜ğ‘€¦, ğ‘€ªğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€”ğ‘€¯ğ‘‚, ğ‘€¢ğ‘€£ğ‘€¸ğ‘€•ğ‘€¢ğ‘„ ğ‘€©ğ‘€¸ğ‘€³ğ‘€¼ğ‘€®ğ‘†ğ‘€®ğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘„, ğ‘€¦ ğ‘€§ğ‘€¥ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¦ğ‘€¯ğ‘€ºğ‘€©ğ‘†ğ‘€ªğ‘€¦ğ‘†ğ‘€¢ğ‘„, ğ‘€¦ ğ‘€†ğ‘€¯ğ‘€¢ğ‘†ğ‘€¢ğ‘„ ğ‘€©ğ‘€¸ğ‘€³ğ‘€¼ğ‘€®ğ‘†ğ‘€®ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¬. ğ‘€…ğ‘€­ğ‘€³ğ‘€, ğ‘€ªğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€”ğ‘€¯ğ‘‚, ğ‘€¢ğ‘€£ğ‘€¸ğ‘€•ğ‘€¢ğ‘„ ğ‘€²ğ‘€«ğ‘†ğ‘€«ğ‘€¸ğ‘€²ğ‘€«ğ‘†ğ‘€©ğ‘€¼ğ‘€¤ğ‘†ğ‘€¥ğ‘„. ğ‘€‘ğ‘€¤ğ‘€³ğ‘€£, ğ‘€ªğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€”ğ‘€¯ğ‘‚, ğ‘€²ğ‘„ğ‘€¢ğ‘€, ğ‘€…ğ‘€«ğ‘€¢ğ‘€«ğ‘€¥ğ‘€ºğ‘€•ğ‘€¢ğ‘€, ğ‘€…ğ‘€³ğ‘€«ğ‘€¦ğ‘€¼ğ‘€²ğ‘€¸ğ‘€²ğ‘€¸ğ‘€«ğ‘€º, ğ‘€…ğ‘€³ğ‘€ ğ‘€¥ğ‘€«ğ‘†ğ‘€«ğ‘€ ğ‘€¤ğ‘‚ğ‘€²ğ‘‚ğ‘€«ğ‘€º. ğ‘€¬ğ‘€£ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¦ğ‘€¼ğ‘€²ğ‘€ºğ‘€ğ‘†ğ‘€ğ‘€ ğ‘€¢ğ‘€£ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€§ğ‘€ğ‘€ºğ‘€§ğ‘€šğ‘†ğ‘€šğ‘€«ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¦ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€¦ğ‘€˜ğ‘€ºğ‘€­ğ‘€²ğ‘†ğ‘€²ğ‘‚ğ‘€¯â€”  ğ‘€¬ğ‘€²ğ‘†ğ‘€²ğ‘€¢ğ‘†ğ‘€£ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¬ ğ‘€“ğ‘€¼ğ‘€®ğ‘€§ğ‘€¼ğ‘€¢ğ‘†ğ‘€¢ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€²ğ‘€«ğ‘†ğ‘€«ğ‘€¤ğ‘‚ğ‘€¯ ğ‘€…ğ‘€•ğ‘€¸ğ‘€­ğ‘€²ğ‘†ğ‘€«ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€…ğ‘€¦ğ‘€•ğ‘€¸ğ‘€­ğ‘€ºğ‘€¬ğ‘€ ğ‘€§ğ‘€©ğ‘†ğ‘€©ğ‘€šğ‘€¦ğ‘†ğ‘€¢ğ‘€º, ğ‘€¢ğ‘€¤ğ‘€¦ğ‘€¼ğ‘€¢ğ‘†ğ‘€¢ğ‘€­ğ‘€â€”  ğ‘€©ğ‘†ğ‘€­ğ‘€³ğ‘†ğ‘€«ğ‘€˜ğ‘€­ğ‘€ºğ‘€¬ğ‘€§ğ‘€­ğ‘€ºğ‘€¬ğ‘„ğ‘€²ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¦ğ‘€ ğ‘€¤ğ‘€ºğ‘€ğ‘†ğ‘€ğ‘‚ğ‘€¯ ğ‘€¥ğ‘€«ğ‘†ğ‘€«ğ‘‚ ğ‘€²ğ‘€¬ğ‘€ ğ‘€…ğ‘€ªğ‘€ºğ‘€œğ‘†ğ‘€œğ‘€¸ ğ‘€²ğ‘€˜ğ‘†ğ‘€™ğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘€¢ğ‘†ğ‘€¯ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€‰ğ‘€§ğ‘€²ğ‘€«ğ‘†ğ‘€§ğ‘€šğ‘†ğ‘€š ğ‘€¯ğ‘€ºğ‘€³ğ‘€­ğ‘€ºğ‘€²ğ‘†ğ‘€²ğ‘€£ğ‘€¸â€™ğ‘€¢ğ‘€º.

989\. ğ‘€¤ğ‘€¼ğ‘€¢ğ‘€ºğ‘€¬ğ‘€«ğ‘†ğ‘€§ğ‘€º ğ‘€”ğ‘„, ğ‘€ªğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€”ğ‘€¯ğ‘‚, ğ‘€§ğ‘€œğ‘†ğ‘€˜ğ‘€¯ğ‘€•ğ‘†ğ‘€•ğ‘€ºğ‘€¬ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€ªğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€”ğ‘€½ ğ‘€«ğ‘€ ğ‘€ğ‘€¢ğ‘€¤ğ‘€¯ğ‘„ğ‘€˜ğ‘€¼ğ‘€â€”  â€˜ğ‘€¢ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¬ğ‘€§ğ‘€º ğ‘€”ğ‘„ ğ‘€¢ğ‘†ğ‘€¯ğ‘€, ğ‘€†ğ‘€¯ğ‘€¼ğ‘€²ğ‘„ ğ‘€•ğ‘„ğ‘€¢ğ‘€«, ğ‘€‡ğ‘€­ğ‘€ºğ‘€¬ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¬ ğ‘€¢ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¬ ğ‘€§ğ‘€ğ‘€ºğ‘€§ğ‘€¤ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¬ ğ‘€¢ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¬ ğ‘€¤ğ‘€¼ğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€“ğ‘€­ğ‘€“ğ‘€¸ğ‘€­ğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¬ ğ‘€¦ğ‘€¸ğ‘€šğ‘†ğ‘€›ğ‘€•ğ‘€«ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€‰ğ‘€¢ğ‘†ğ‘€¢ğ‘€­ğ‘€º ğ‘€«ğ‘€¦ğ‘€¼ğ‘€²ğ‘†ğ‘€²ğ‘€¥ğ‘€«ğ‘†ğ‘€«ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€…ğ‘€®ğ‘€«ğ‘€­ğ‘€ºğ‘€¬ğ‘€œğ‘€¸ğ‘€¡ğ‘€¤ğ‘€²ğ‘†ğ‘€²ğ‘€¦ğ‘€¯ğ‘€ºğ‘€²ğ‘‚ğ‘€²ğ‘€, ğ‘€“ğ‘€ºğ‘€ ğ‘€§ğ‘€¦ ğ‘€¢ğ‘†ğ‘€¯ğ‘€ ğ‘€ğ‘€¢ğ‘€­ğ‘€³ğ‘€º ğ‘€©ğ‘€¸ğ‘€³ğ‘€¼ğ‘€®ğ‘†ğ‘€®ğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘„ ğ‘€§ğ‘€¥ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¦ğ‘€¯ğ‘€ºğ‘€©ğ‘†ğ‘€ªğ‘€¦ğ‘†ğ‘€¢ğ‘„ ğ‘€†ğ‘€¯ğ‘€¢ğ‘†ğ‘€¢ğ‘„ ğ‘€©ğ‘€¸ğ‘€³ğ‘€¼ğ‘€®ğ‘†ğ‘€®ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¬ ğ‘€…ğ‘€¥ğ‘€ºğ‘€•ğ‘€«ğ‘€ºğ‘€²ğ‘†ğ‘€²ğ‘€²ğ‘€º ğ‘€‰ğ‘€¢ğ‘†ğ‘€¢ğ‘€­ğ‘€º ğ‘€«ğ‘€¦ğ‘€¼ğ‘€²ğ‘†ğ‘€²ğ‘€¥ğ‘€«ğ‘†ğ‘€«ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€…ğ‘€®ğ‘€«ğ‘€­ğ‘€ºğ‘€¬ğ‘€œğ‘€¸ğ‘€¡ğ‘€¤ğ‘€²ğ‘†ğ‘€²ğ‘€¦ğ‘€¯ğ‘€ºğ‘€²ğ‘‚ğ‘€²ğ‘€¦ğ‘†â€™ğ‘€¢ğ‘€º? ğ‘€¤ğ‘€¼ğ‘€¢ğ‘€ºğ‘€¬ğ‘€«ğ‘†ğ‘€§ğ‘€º ğ‘€”ğ‘„ ğ‘€…ğ‘€³ğ‘€, ğ‘€ªğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€”ğ‘€¯ğ‘‚, ğ‘€§ğ‘€œğ‘†ğ‘€˜ğ‘€¯ğ‘€•ğ‘†ğ‘€•ğ‘€ºğ‘€¬ğ‘‚ ğ‘€ªğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€”ğ‘€½ ğ‘€ğ‘€¢ğ‘€¤ğ‘€¯ğ‘„ğ‘€˜ğ‘€â€”  â€˜ğ‘€¦, ğ‘€ªğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€”ğ‘€¯ğ‘‚, ğ‘€¢ğ‘€£ğ‘€¸ğ‘€•ğ‘€¢ğ‘„ ğ‘€©ğ‘€¸ğ‘€³ğ‘€¼ğ‘€®ğ‘†ğ‘€®ğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘„â€¦ğ‘€§ğ‘‚â€¦  ğ‘€‰ğ‘€§ğ‘€²ğ‘€«ğ‘†ğ‘€§ğ‘€šğ‘†ğ‘€š ğ‘€¯ğ‘€ºğ‘€³ğ‘€­ğ‘€ºğ‘€²ğ‘†ğ‘€²ğ‘€£ğ‘€¸â€™ğ‘€¢ğ‘€º.

## The Discourse

<Tabs syncKey="paliquote">
<TabItem label="My Translation">
994\. There are, bhikkhave, these five strands of sensual pleasure. What are the five? Forms cognizable by the eyeâ€”agreeable, pleasant, charming, alluring, accompanied by sensual desire, and stimulating; sounds cognizable by the earâ€”agreeable, pleasant, charming, alluring, accompanied by sensual desire, and stimulating; smells cognizable by the noseâ€”agreeable, pleasant, charming, alluring, accompanied by sensual desire, and stimulating; tastes cognizable by the tongueâ€”agreeable, pleasant, charming, alluring, accompanied by sensual desire, and stimulating; tactile objects cognizable by the bodyâ€”agreeable, pleasant, charming, alluring, accompanied by sensual desire, and stimulating. These, bhikkhave, are the five strands of sensual pleasure. Whatever recluses or brahmins, bhikkhave, are entangled, infatuated, and engrossed in these five strands of sensual pleasure, not seeing their danger and having no understanding of their escape, they should be known as being â€˜fallen into disaster, fallen into calamity, subject to the Evil Oneâ€™s willâ€™.

995\. Just as, bhikkhave, a wild deer, bound, might lie down on a heap of snares. It should be known as being â€˜fallen into disaster, fallen into calamity, subject to the hunterâ€™s will. And when the hunter comes, it will not be able to go wherever it wants.â€™ Exactly in the same way, bhikkhave, whatever recluses or brahmins are entangled, infatuated, and engrossed in these five strands of sensual pleasure, not seeing their danger and having no understanding of their escape, they should be known as being â€˜fallen into disaster, fallen into calamity, subject to the Evil Oneâ€™s willâ€™. But whatever recluses or brahmins, bhikkhave, are not entangled, not infatuated, and not engrossed in these five strands of sensual pleasure, seeing their danger and having an understanding of their escape, they should be known as being â€˜not fallen into disaster, not fallen into calamity, not subject to the Evil Oneâ€™s willâ€™.

996\. Just as, bhikkhave, a wild deer, unbound, might lie down on a heap of snares. It should be known as being â€˜not fallen into disaster, not fallen into calamity, not subject to the hunterâ€™s will. And when the hunter comes, it will be able to go wherever it wants.â€™ Exactly in the same way, bhikkhave, whatever recluses or brahmins are not entangled, not infatuated, and not engrossed in these five strands of sensual pleasure, seeing their danger and having an understanding of their escape, they should be known as being â€˜not fallen into disaster, not fallen into calamity, not subject to the Evil Oneâ€™s willâ€™.

997\. Just as, bhikkhave, a wild deer roaming in the wilderness, goes confidently, stands confidently, sits confidently, sleeps confidently. For what reason? It is beyond the reach of the hunter, bhikkhave. In the same way, bhikkhave, a monk, quite secluded from sensual pleasures, secluded from unwholesome states, enters and dwells in the first jhÄna, which is accompanied by applied thought and sustained thought, with rapture and pleasure born of seclusion. This, bhikkhave, is called a monk who has blinded MÄra, made him footless, and having struck out MÄraâ€™s eye, has gone beyond the sight of the Evil One.

998\. Furthermore, bhikkhave, a monk, with the stilling of applied thought and sustained thought, with the internal pacification and unification of mind, enters and dwells in the second jhÄna, which is without applied thought and sustained thought, with rapture and pleasure born of concentration. This, bhikkhave, is called a monk who has blinded MÄra, made him footless, and having struck out MÄraâ€™s eye, has gone beyond the sight of the Evil One.

999\. Furthermore, bhikkhave, a monk, with the fading away of rapture, dwells in equanimity, mindful and clearly comprehending, and experiences pleasure with the body, of which the noble ones declare: â€˜He dwells in pleasure with equanimity and mindfulness.â€™ He enters and dwells in the third jhÄna. This, bhikkhave, is called a monk who has blinded MÄra, made him footless, and having struck out MÄraâ€™s eye, has gone beyond the sight of the Evil One.

1000\. Furthermore, bhikkhave, a monk, with the abandoning of pleasure and pain, and with the prior disappearance of joy and grief, enters and dwells in the fourth jhÄna, which is neither painful nor pleasant and is purified by equanimity and mindfulness. This, bhikkhave, is called a monk who has blinded MÄra, made him footless, and having struck out MÄraâ€™s eye, has gone beyond the sight of the Evil One.

1001\. Furthermore, bhikkhave, a monk, by completely surmounting perceptions of form, by the disappearance of perceptions of sensory impact, by not attending to perceptions of diversity, thinking: â€˜Space is infinite,â€™ enters and dwells in the sphere of infinite space. This, bhikkhave, is called a monk who has blinded MÄra, made him footless, and having struck out MÄraâ€™s eye, has gone beyond the sight of the Evil One.

1002\. Furthermore, bhikkhave, a monk, by completely surmounting the sphere of infinite space, thinking: â€˜Consciousness is infinite,â€™ enters and dwells in the sphere of infinite consciousness. This, bhikkhave, is called a monk who has blinded MÄra, made him footless, and having struck out MÄraâ€™s eye, has gone beyond the sight of the Evil One.

1003\. Furthermore, bhikkhave, a monk, by completely surmounting the sphere of infinite consciousness, thinking: â€˜There is nothing,â€™ enters and dwells in the sphere of nothingness. This, bhikkhave, is called a monk who has blinded MÄra, made him footless, and having struck out MÄraâ€™s eye, has gone beyond the sight of the Evil One.

1004\. Furthermore, bhikkhave, a monk, by completely surmounting the sphere of nothingness, enters and dwells in the sphere of neither-perception-nor-non-perception. This, bhikkhave, is called a monk who has blinded MÄra, made him footless, and having struck out MÄraâ€™s eye, has gone beyond the sight of the Evil One.

1005\. Furthermore, bhikkhave, a monk, by completely surmounting the sphere of neither-perception-nor-non-perception, enters and dwells in the cessation of perception and feeling. And having seen with wisdom, his defilements are destroyed. This, bhikkhave, is called a monk who has blinded MÄra, made him footless, and having struck out MÄraâ€™s eye, has gone beyond the sight of the Evil One. Having crossed over the entanglement in the world, he goes confidently, stands confidently, sits confidently, sleeps confidently. For what reason? He is beyond the reach of the Evil One, bhikkhave.â€

1006\. The BhagavÄ spoke this. Delighted, those bhikkhave rejoiced in the BhagavÄâ€™s words.

1007\. The Discourse on the Heap of Snares, the sixth, is finished.

996\. ğ‘€²ğ‘‚ğ‘€¬ğ‘†ğ‘€¬ğ‘€£ğ‘€¸ğ‘€§ğ‘€º, ğ‘€ªğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€”ğ‘€¯ğ‘‚, ğ‘€†ğ‘€­ğ‘€œğ‘†ğ‘€œğ‘€“ğ‘„ ğ‘€«ğ‘€•ğ‘„ ğ‘€…ğ‘€©ğ‘€¤ğ‘†ğ‘€¥ğ‘„ ğ‘€§ğ‘€¸ğ‘€²ğ‘€­ğ‘€¸ğ‘€²ğ‘€ºğ‘€ ğ‘€…ğ‘€¥ğ‘€ºğ‘€²ğ‘€¬ğ‘‚ğ‘€¬ğ‘†ğ‘€¬. ğ‘€²ğ‘„ ğ‘€ğ‘€¯ğ‘€«ğ‘€²ğ‘†ğ‘€² ğ‘€¯ğ‘‚ğ‘€¤ğ‘€ºğ‘€¢ğ‘€©ğ‘†ğ‘€©ğ‘„â€”  â€˜ğ‘€¦ ğ‘€…ğ‘€¦ğ‘€¬ğ‘€«ğ‘€¸ğ‘€§ğ‘€¦ğ‘†ğ‘€¦ğ‘„ ğ‘€¦ ğ‘€©ğ‘†ğ‘€¬ğ‘€²ğ‘€¦ğ‘€«ğ‘€¸ğ‘€§ğ‘€¦ğ‘†ğ‘€¦ğ‘„ ğ‘€¦ ğ‘€¬ğ‘€£ğ‘€¸ğ‘€“ğ‘€¸ğ‘€«ğ‘€“ğ‘€­ğ‘€¡ğ‘€»ğ‘€¬ğ‘„ ğ‘€®ğ‘€¼ğ‘€¤ğ‘†ğ‘€¤ğ‘€²ğ‘†ğ‘€². ğ‘€†ğ‘€•ğ‘€˜ğ‘†ğ‘€™ğ‘€¦ğ‘†ğ‘€¢ğ‘‚ ğ‘€˜ ğ‘€§ğ‘€¦ ğ‘€®ğ‘€¼ğ‘€¤ğ‘†ğ‘€¤ğ‘‚ ğ‘€¬ğ‘‚ğ‘€¦ ğ‘€“ğ‘€¸ğ‘€«ğ‘€ ğ‘€§ğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€“ğ‘€«ğ‘€ºğ‘€²ğ‘†ğ‘€²ğ‘€¢ğ‘€»â€™ğ‘€¢ğ‘€º. ğ‘€ğ‘€¯ğ‘€«ğ‘‚ğ‘€¯ ğ‘€”ğ‘„, ğ‘€ªğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€”ğ‘€¯ğ‘‚, ğ‘€¬ğ‘‚ ğ‘€³ğ‘€º ğ‘€“ğ‘‚ğ‘€˜ğ‘€º ğ‘€²ğ‘€«ğ‘€¡ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€¯ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€©ğ‘†ğ‘€­ğ‘€¸ğ‘€³ğ‘†ğ‘€«ğ‘€¡ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€¯ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€‡ğ‘€«ğ‘‚ ğ‘€§ğ‘€œğ‘†ğ‘€˜ ğ‘€“ğ‘€¸ğ‘€«ğ‘€•ğ‘€¼ğ‘€¡ğ‘‚ ğ‘€…ğ‘€•ğ‘€£ğ‘€ºğ‘€¢ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€…ğ‘€«ğ‘€¼ğ‘€˜ğ‘†ğ‘€™ğ‘€ºğ‘€¢ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€…ğ‘€¦ğ‘€šğ‘†ğ‘€›ğ‘„ğ‘€§ğ‘€¦ğ‘†ğ‘€¦ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€†ğ‘€¤ğ‘€»ğ‘€¦ğ‘€¯ğ‘€¤ğ‘€²ğ‘†ğ‘€²ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¯ğ‘€ºğ‘€¦ğ‘„ ğ‘€¦ğ‘€ºğ‘€²ğ‘†ğ‘€²ğ‘€­ğ‘€¡ğ‘€§ğ‘€œğ‘†ğ‘€œğ‘€¸ ğ‘€§ğ‘€­ğ‘€ºğ‘€ªğ‘€¼ğ‘€œğ‘†ğ‘€šğ‘€¦ğ‘†ğ‘€¢ğ‘€º, ğ‘€¢ğ‘‚ ğ‘€ğ‘€¯ğ‘€«ğ‘€²ğ‘†ğ‘€²ğ‘€¼ ğ‘€¯ğ‘‚ğ‘€¤ğ‘€ºğ‘€¢ğ‘€©ğ‘†ğ‘€©ğ‘€¸â€”  â€˜ğ‘€¦ ğ‘€…ğ‘€¦ğ‘€¬ğ‘€«ğ‘€¸ğ‘€§ğ‘€¦ğ‘†ğ‘€¦ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€¦ ğ‘€©ğ‘†ğ‘€¬ğ‘€²ğ‘€¦ğ‘€«ğ‘€¸ğ‘€§ğ‘€¦ğ‘†ğ‘€¦ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€¦ ğ‘€¬ğ‘€£ğ‘€¸ğ‘€“ğ‘€¸ğ‘€«ğ‘€“ğ‘€­ğ‘€¡ğ‘€»ğ‘€¬ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€§ğ‘€¸ğ‘€§ğ‘€ºğ‘€«ğ‘€¢ğ‘„â€™.

997\. ğ‘€²ğ‘‚ğ‘€¬ğ‘†ğ‘€¬ğ‘€£ğ‘€¸ğ‘€§ğ‘€º, ğ‘€ªğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€”ğ‘€¯ğ‘‚, ğ‘€†ğ‘€­ğ‘€œğ‘†ğ‘€œğ‘€“ğ‘„ ğ‘€«ğ‘€•ğ‘„ ğ‘€…ğ‘€­ğ‘€œğ‘†ğ‘€œğ‘‚ ğ‘€§ğ‘€¯ğ‘€¦ğ‘‚ ğ‘€˜ğ‘€­ğ‘€«ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¦ğ‘„ ğ‘€¯ğ‘€ºğ‘€²ğ‘†ğ‘€²ğ‘€¢ğ‘†ğ‘€£ğ‘„ ğ‘€•ğ‘€˜ğ‘†ğ‘€™ğ‘€¢ğ‘€º, ğ‘€¯ğ‘€ºğ‘€²ğ‘†ğ‘€²ğ‘€¢ğ‘†ğ‘€£ğ‘„ ğ‘€¢ğ‘€ºğ‘€ğ‘†ğ‘€ğ‘€¢ğ‘€º, ğ‘€¯ğ‘€ºğ‘€²ğ‘†ğ‘€²ğ‘€¢ğ‘†ğ‘€£ğ‘„ ğ‘€¦ğ‘€ºğ‘€²ğ‘€»ğ‘€¤ğ‘€¢ğ‘€º, ğ‘€¯ğ‘€ºğ‘€²ğ‘†ğ‘€²ğ‘€¢ğ‘†ğ‘€£ğ‘„ ğ‘€²ğ‘‚ğ‘€¬ğ‘†ğ‘€¬ğ‘€ ğ‘€“ğ‘€§ğ‘†ğ‘€§ğ‘‚ğ‘€¢ğ‘€º. ğ‘€¢ğ‘€ ğ‘€“ğ‘€ºğ‘€²ğ‘†ğ‘€² ğ‘€³ğ‘‚ğ‘€¢ğ‘€¼? ğ‘€…ğ‘€¦ğ‘€¸ğ‘€§ğ‘€¸ğ‘€£ğ‘€•ğ‘€¢ğ‘„, ğ‘€ªğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€”ğ‘€¯ğ‘‚, ğ‘€®ğ‘€¼ğ‘€¤ğ‘†ğ‘€¤ğ‘€²ğ‘†ğ‘€². ğ‘€ğ‘€¯ğ‘€«ğ‘‚ğ‘€¯ ğ‘€”ğ‘„, ğ‘€ªğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€”ğ‘€¯ğ‘‚, ğ‘€ªğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€”ğ‘€¼ ğ‘€¯ğ‘€ºğ‘€¯ğ‘€ºğ‘€˜ğ‘†ğ‘€˜ğ‘‚ğ‘€¯ ğ‘€“ğ‘€¸ğ‘€«ğ‘‚ğ‘€³ğ‘€º ğ‘€¯ğ‘€ºğ‘€¯ğ‘€ºğ‘€˜ğ‘†ğ‘€˜ ğ‘€…ğ‘€“ğ‘€¼ğ‘€²ğ‘€®ğ‘‚ğ‘€³ğ‘€º ğ‘€¥ğ‘€«ğ‘†ğ‘€«ğ‘‚ğ‘€³ğ‘€º ğ‘€²ğ‘€¯ğ‘€ºğ‘€¢ğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€“ğ‘€ ğ‘€²ğ‘€¯ğ‘€ºğ‘€˜ğ‘€¸ğ‘€­ğ‘€ ğ‘€¯ğ‘€ºğ‘€¯ğ‘‚ğ‘€“ğ‘€šğ‘€ ğ‘€§ğ‘€»ğ‘€¢ğ‘€ºğ‘€²ğ‘€¼ğ‘€”ğ‘€ ğ‘€§ğ‘€ğ‘€«ğ‘€ ğ‘€›ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¦ğ‘€ ğ‘€‰ğ‘€§ğ‘€²ğ‘€«ğ‘†ğ‘€§ğ‘€šğ‘†ğ‘€š ğ‘€¯ğ‘€ºğ‘€³ğ‘€­ğ‘€¢ğ‘€º. ğ‘€…ğ‘€¬ğ‘€ ğ‘€¯ğ‘€¼ğ‘€˜ğ‘†ğ‘€˜ğ‘€¢ğ‘€º, ğ‘€ªğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€”ğ‘€¯ğ‘‚, ğ‘€ªğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€”ğ‘€¼ ğ‘€…ğ‘€¦ğ‘†ğ‘€¥ğ‘€«ğ‘€“ğ‘€¸ğ‘€²ğ‘€º ğ‘€«ğ‘€¸ğ‘€­ğ‘€ ğ‘€…ğ‘€§ğ‘€¤ğ‘€, ğ‘€¯ğ‘€¥ğ‘€ºğ‘€¢ğ‘†ğ‘€¯ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€«ğ‘€¸ğ‘€­ğ‘€˜ğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€”ğ‘€¼ğ‘€ ğ‘€…ğ‘€¤ğ‘€²ğ‘†ğ‘€²ğ‘€¦ğ‘€ ğ‘€•ğ‘€¢ğ‘„ ğ‘€§ğ‘€¸ğ‘€§ğ‘€ºğ‘€«ğ‘€¢ğ‘„.

998\. ğ‘€§ğ‘€¼ğ‘€¦ ğ‘€˜ğ‘€§ğ‘€­ğ‘€, ğ‘€ªğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€”ğ‘€¯ğ‘‚, ğ‘€ªğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€”ğ‘€¼ ğ‘€¯ğ‘€ºğ‘€¢ğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€“ğ‘€¯ğ‘€ºğ‘€˜ğ‘€¸ğ‘€­ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¦ğ‘€ ğ‘€¯ğ‘€½ğ‘€§ğ‘€²ğ‘€«ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€…ğ‘€šğ‘†ğ‘€›ğ‘€¢ğ‘†ğ‘€¢ğ‘€ ğ‘€²ğ‘€«ğ‘†ğ‘€§ğ‘€²ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¤ğ‘€¦ğ‘€ ğ‘€˜ğ‘‚ğ‘€¢ğ‘€²ğ‘„ ğ‘€ğ‘€“ğ‘„ğ‘€¤ğ‘€ºğ‘€ªğ‘€¸ğ‘€¯ğ‘€ ğ‘€…ğ‘€¯ğ‘€ºğ‘€¢ğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€“ğ‘€ ğ‘€…ğ‘€¯ğ‘€ºğ‘€˜ğ‘€¸ğ‘€­ğ‘€ ğ‘€²ğ‘€«ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¥ğ‘€ºğ‘€šğ‘€ ğ‘€§ğ‘€»ğ‘€¢ğ‘€ºğ‘€²ğ‘€¼ğ‘€”ğ‘€ ğ‘€¤ğ‘€¼ğ‘€¢ğ‘€ºğ‘€¬ğ‘€ ğ‘€›ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¦ğ‘€ ğ‘€‰ğ‘€§ğ‘€²ğ‘€«ğ‘†ğ‘€§ğ‘€šğ‘†ğ‘€š ğ‘€¯ğ‘€ºğ‘€³ğ‘€­ğ‘€¢ğ‘€º. ğ‘€…ğ‘€¬ğ‘€ ğ‘€¯ğ‘€¼ğ‘€˜ğ‘†ğ‘€˜ğ‘€¢ğ‘€º, ğ‘€ªğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€”ğ‘€¯ğ‘‚â€¦ğ‘€§ğ‘‚â€¦  ğ‘€§ğ‘€¸ğ‘€§ğ‘€ºğ‘€«ğ‘€¢ğ‘„.

999\. ğ‘€§ğ‘€¼ğ‘€¦ ğ‘€˜ğ‘€§ğ‘€­ğ‘€, ğ‘€ªğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€”ğ‘€¯ğ‘‚, ğ‘€ªğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€”ğ‘€¼ ğ‘€§ğ‘€»ğ‘€¢ğ‘€ºğ‘€¬ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€˜ ğ‘€¯ğ‘€ºğ‘€­ğ‘€¸ğ‘€•ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€‰ğ‘€§ğ‘‚ğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€”ğ‘€“ğ‘„ ğ‘€˜ ğ‘€¯ğ‘€ºğ‘€³ğ‘€­ğ‘€¢ğ‘€º, ğ‘€²ğ‘€¢ğ‘„ ğ‘€˜ ğ‘€²ğ‘€«ğ‘†ğ‘€§ğ‘€šğ‘€¸ğ‘€¦ğ‘„, ğ‘€²ğ‘€¼ğ‘€”ğ‘€œğ‘†ğ‘€˜ ğ‘€“ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¬ğ‘‚ğ‘€¦ ğ‘€§ğ‘€ğ‘€ºğ‘€²ğ‘€ğ‘€¯ğ‘‚ğ‘€¤ğ‘‚ğ‘€¢ğ‘€º ğ‘€¬ğ‘€ ğ‘€¢ğ‘€ ğ‘€…ğ‘€­ğ‘€ºğ‘€¬ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€†ğ‘€˜ğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€”ğ‘€¦ğ‘†ğ‘€¢ğ‘€º â€˜ğ‘€‰ğ‘€§ğ‘‚ğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€”ğ‘€“ğ‘„ ğ‘€²ğ‘€¢ğ‘€ºğ‘€«ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€²ğ‘€¼ğ‘€”ğ‘€¯ğ‘€ºğ‘€³ğ‘€¸ğ‘€­ğ‘€»â€™ğ‘€¢ğ‘€º ğ‘€¢ğ‘€¢ğ‘€ºğ‘€¬ğ‘€ ğ‘€›ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¦ğ‘€ ğ‘€‰ğ‘€§ğ‘€²ğ‘€«ğ‘†ğ‘€§ğ‘€šğ‘†ğ‘€š ğ‘€¯ğ‘€ºğ‘€³ğ‘€­ğ‘€¢ğ‘€º. ğ‘€…ğ‘€¬ğ‘€ ğ‘€¯ğ‘€¼ğ‘€˜ğ‘†ğ‘€˜ğ‘€¢ğ‘€º, ğ‘€ªğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€”ğ‘€¯ğ‘‚â€¦ğ‘€§ğ‘‚â€¦  ğ‘€§ğ‘€¸ğ‘€§ğ‘€ºğ‘€«ğ‘€¢ğ‘„.

1000\. ğ‘€§ğ‘€¼ğ‘€¦ ğ‘€˜ğ‘€§ğ‘€­ğ‘€, ğ‘€ªğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€”ğ‘€¯ğ‘‚, ğ‘€ªğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€”ğ‘€¼ ğ‘€²ğ‘€¼ğ‘€”ğ‘€²ğ‘†ğ‘€² ğ‘€˜ ğ‘€§ğ‘€³ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¦ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€¤ğ‘€¼ğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€”ğ‘€²ğ‘†ğ‘€² ğ‘€˜ ğ‘€§ğ‘€³ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¦ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€§ğ‘€¼ğ‘€©ğ‘†ğ‘€©ğ‘‚ğ‘€¯ ğ‘€²ğ‘„ğ‘€«ğ‘€¦ğ‘€²ğ‘†ğ‘€²ğ‘€¤ğ‘„ğ‘€«ğ‘€¦ğ‘€²ğ‘†ğ‘€²ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¦ğ‘€ ğ‘€…ğ‘€¢ğ‘†ğ‘€£ğ‘€—ğ‘†ğ‘€•ğ‘€«ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€…ğ‘€¤ğ‘€¼ğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€”ğ‘€«ğ‘€²ğ‘€¼ğ‘€”ğ‘€ ğ‘€‰ğ‘€§ğ‘‚ğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€”ğ‘€¸ğ‘€²ğ‘€¢ğ‘€ºğ‘€§ğ‘€¸ğ‘€­ğ‘€ºğ‘€²ğ‘€¼ğ‘€¤ğ‘†ğ‘€¥ğ‘€ºğ‘€ ğ‘€˜ğ‘€¢ğ‘€¼ğ‘€¢ğ‘†ğ‘€£ğ‘€ ğ‘€›ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¦ğ‘€ ğ‘€‰ğ‘€§ğ‘€²ğ‘€«ğ‘†ğ‘€§ğ‘€šğ‘†ğ‘€š ğ‘€¯ğ‘€ºğ‘€³ğ‘€­ğ‘€¢ğ‘€º. ğ‘€…ğ‘€¬ğ‘€ ğ‘€¯ğ‘€¼ğ‘€˜ğ‘†ğ‘€˜ğ‘€¢ğ‘€º, ğ‘€ªğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€”ğ‘€¯ğ‘‚â€¦ğ‘€§ğ‘‚â€¦  ğ‘€§ğ‘€¸ğ‘€§ğ‘€ºğ‘€«ğ‘€¢ğ‘„.

1001\. ğ‘€§ğ‘€¼ğ‘€¦ ğ‘€˜ğ‘€§ğ‘€­ğ‘€, ğ‘€ªğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€”ğ‘€¯ğ‘‚, ğ‘€ªğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€”ğ‘€¼ ğ‘€²ğ‘€©ğ‘†ğ‘€©ğ‘€²ğ‘„ ğ‘€­ğ‘€½ğ‘€§ğ‘€²ğ‘€œğ‘†ğ‘€œğ‘€¸ğ‘€¦ğ‘€ ğ‘€²ğ‘€«ğ‘€¢ğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€“ğ‘€«ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€§ğ‘€ğ‘€ºğ‘€–ğ‘€²ğ‘€œğ‘†ğ‘€œğ‘€¸ğ‘€¦ğ‘€ ğ‘€…ğ‘€¢ğ‘†ğ‘€£ğ‘€—ğ‘†ğ‘€•ğ‘€«ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€¦ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¦ğ‘€¢ğ‘†ğ‘€¢ğ‘€²ğ‘€œğ‘†ğ‘€œğ‘€¸ğ‘€¦ğ‘€ ğ‘€…ğ‘€«ğ‘€¦ğ‘€²ğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘€¸ğ‘€­ğ‘€¸ â€˜ğ‘€…ğ‘€¦ğ‘€¦ğ‘†ğ‘€¢ğ‘„ ğ‘€†ğ‘€“ğ‘€¸ğ‘€²ğ‘„â€™ğ‘€¢ğ‘€º ğ‘€†ğ‘€“ğ‘€¸ğ‘€²ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¦ğ‘€œğ‘†ğ‘€˜ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¬ğ‘€¢ğ‘€¦ğ‘€ ğ‘€‰ğ‘€§ğ‘€²ğ‘€«ğ‘†ğ‘€§ğ‘€šğ‘†ğ‘€š ğ‘€¯ğ‘€ºğ‘€³ğ‘€­ğ‘€¢ğ‘€º. ğ‘€…ğ‘€¬ğ‘€ ğ‘€¯ğ‘€¼ğ‘€˜ğ‘†ğ‘€˜ğ‘€¢ğ‘€º, ğ‘€ªğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€”ğ‘€¯ğ‘‚â€¦ğ‘€§ğ‘‚â€¦  ğ‘€§ğ‘€¸ğ‘€§ğ‘€ºğ‘€«ğ‘€¢ğ‘„.

1002\. ğ‘€§ğ‘€¼ğ‘€¦ ğ‘€˜ğ‘€§ğ‘€­ğ‘€, ğ‘€ªğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€”ğ‘€¯ğ‘‚, ğ‘€ªğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€”ğ‘€¼ ğ‘€²ğ‘€©ğ‘†ğ‘€©ğ‘€²ğ‘„ ğ‘€†ğ‘€“ğ‘€¸ğ‘€²ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¦ğ‘€œğ‘†ğ‘€˜ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¬ğ‘€¢ğ‘€¦ğ‘€ ğ‘€²ğ‘€«ğ‘€¢ğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€“ğ‘€«ğ‘†ğ‘€« â€˜ğ‘€…ğ‘€¦ğ‘€¦ğ‘†ğ‘€¢ğ‘€ ğ‘€¯ğ‘€ºğ‘€œğ‘†ğ‘€œğ‘€¸ğ‘€¡ğ‘€¦ğ‘†â€™ğ‘€¢ğ‘€º ğ‘€¯ğ‘€ºğ‘€œğ‘†ğ‘€œğ‘€¸ğ‘€¡ğ‘€œğ‘†ğ‘€˜ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¬ğ‘€¢ğ‘€¦ğ‘€ ğ‘€‰ğ‘€§ğ‘€²ğ‘€«ğ‘†ğ‘€§ğ‘€šğ‘†ğ‘€š ğ‘€¯ğ‘€ºğ‘€³ğ‘€­ğ‘€¢ğ‘€º. ğ‘€…ğ‘€¬ğ‘€ ğ‘€¯ğ‘€¼ğ‘€˜ğ‘†ğ‘€˜ğ‘€¢ğ‘€º, ğ‘€ªğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€”ğ‘€¯ğ‘‚â€¦ğ‘€§ğ‘‚â€¦  ğ‘€§ğ‘€¸ğ‘€§ğ‘€ºğ‘€«ğ‘€¢ğ‘„.

1003\. ğ‘€§ğ‘€¼ğ‘€¦ ğ‘€˜ğ‘€§ğ‘€­ğ‘€, ğ‘€ªğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€”ğ‘€¯ğ‘‚, ğ‘€ªğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€”ğ‘€¼ ğ‘€²ğ‘€©ğ‘†ğ‘€©ğ‘€²ğ‘„ ğ‘€¯ğ‘€ºğ‘€œğ‘†ğ‘€œğ‘€¸ğ‘€¡ğ‘€œğ‘†ğ‘€˜ğ‘€¸ğ‘€¬ğ‘€¢ğ‘€¦ğ‘€ ğ‘€²ğ‘€«ğ‘€¢ğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€“ğ‘€«ğ‘†ğ‘€« â€˜ğ‘€¦ğ‘€¢ğ‘†ğ‘€£ğ‘€º ğ‘€“ğ‘€ºğ‘€œğ‘†ğ‘€˜ğ‘€»â€™ğ‘€¢ğ‘€º ğ‘€†ğ‘€“ğ‘€ºğ‘€œğ‘†ğ‘€˜ğ‘€œğ‘†ğ‘€œğ‘€¸ğ‘€¬ğ‘€¢ğ‘€¦ğ‘€ ğ‘€‰ğ‘€§ğ‘€²ğ‘€«ğ‘†ğ‘€§ğ‘€šğ‘†ğ‘€š ğ‘€¯ğ‘€ºğ‘€³ğ‘€­ğ‘€¢ğ‘€º. ğ‘€…ğ‘€¬ğ‘€ ğ‘€¯ğ‘€¼ğ‘€˜ğ‘†ğ‘€˜ğ‘€¢ğ‘€º, ğ‘€ªğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€”ğ‘€¯ğ‘‚â€¦ğ‘€§ğ‘‚â€¦  ğ‘€§ğ‘€¸ğ‘€§ğ‘€ºğ‘€«ğ‘€¢ğ‘„.

1004\. ğ‘€§ğ‘€¼ğ‘€¦ ğ‘€˜ğ‘€§ğ‘€­ğ‘€, ğ‘€ªğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€”ğ‘€¯ğ‘‚, ğ‘€ªğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€”ğ‘€¼ ğ‘€²ğ‘€©ğ‘†ğ‘€©ğ‘€²ğ‘„ ğ‘€†ğ‘€“ğ‘€ºğ‘€œğ‘†ğ‘€˜ğ‘€œğ‘†ğ‘€œğ‘€¸ğ‘€¬ğ‘€¢ğ‘€¦ğ‘€ ğ‘€²ğ‘€«ğ‘€¢ğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€“ğ‘€«ğ‘†ğ‘€« ğ‘€¦ğ‘‚ğ‘€¯ğ‘€²ğ‘€œğ‘†ğ‘€œğ‘€¸ğ‘€¦ğ‘€¸ğ‘€²ğ‘€œğ‘†ğ‘€œğ‘€¸ğ‘€¬ğ‘€¢ğ‘€¦ğ‘€ ğ‘€‰ğ‘€§ğ‘€²ğ‘€«ğ‘†ğ‘€§ğ‘€šğ‘†ğ‘€š ğ‘€¯ğ‘€ºğ‘€³ğ‘€­ğ‘€¢ğ‘€º. ğ‘€…ğ‘€¬ğ‘€ ğ‘€¯ğ‘€¼ğ‘€˜ğ‘†ğ‘€˜ğ‘€¢ğ‘€º, ğ‘€ªğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€”ğ‘€¯ğ‘‚â€¦ğ‘€§ğ‘‚â€¦  ğ‘€§ğ‘€¸ğ‘€§ğ‘€ºğ‘€«ğ‘€¢ğ‘„.

1005\. ğ‘€§ğ‘€¼ğ‘€¦ ğ‘€˜ğ‘€§ğ‘€­ğ‘€, ğ‘€ªğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€”ğ‘€¯ğ‘‚, ğ‘€ªğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€”ğ‘€¼ ğ‘€²ğ‘€©ğ‘†ğ‘€©ğ‘€²ğ‘„ ğ‘€¦ğ‘‚ğ‘€¯ğ‘€²ğ‘€œğ‘†ğ‘€œğ‘€¸ğ‘€¦ğ‘€¸ğ‘€²ğ‘€œğ‘†ğ‘€œğ‘€¸ğ‘€¬ğ‘€¢ğ‘€¦ğ‘€ ğ‘€²ğ‘€«ğ‘€¢ğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€“ğ‘€«ğ‘†ğ‘€« ğ‘€²ğ‘€œğ‘†ğ‘€œğ‘€¸ğ‘€¯ğ‘‚ğ‘€¤ğ‘€¬ğ‘€ºğ‘€¢ğ‘€¦ğ‘€ºğ‘€­ğ‘„ğ‘€¥ğ‘€ ğ‘€‰ğ‘€§ğ‘€²ğ‘€«ğ‘†ğ‘€§ğ‘€šğ‘†ğ‘€š ğ‘€¯ğ‘€ºğ‘€³ğ‘€­ğ‘€¢ğ‘€º, ğ‘€§ğ‘€œğ‘†ğ‘€œğ‘€¸ğ‘€¬ ğ‘€˜ğ‘€²ğ‘†ğ‘€² ğ‘€¤ğ‘€ºğ‘€²ğ‘†ğ‘€¯ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€†ğ‘€²ğ‘€¯ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€§ğ‘€­ğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€”ğ‘€»ğ‘€¡ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€³ğ‘„ğ‘€¦ğ‘†ğ‘€¢ğ‘€º. ğ‘€…ğ‘€¬ğ‘€ ğ‘€¯ğ‘€¼ğ‘€˜ğ‘†ğ‘€˜ğ‘€¢ğ‘€º, ğ‘€ªğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€”ğ‘€¯ğ‘‚, ğ‘€ªğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€”ğ‘€¼ ğ‘€…ğ‘€¦ğ‘†ğ‘€¥ğ‘€«ğ‘€“ğ‘€¸ğ‘€²ğ‘€º ğ‘€«ğ‘€¸ğ‘€­ğ‘€ ğ‘€…ğ‘€§ğ‘€¤ğ‘€, ğ‘€¯ğ‘€¥ğ‘€ºğ‘€¢ğ‘†ğ‘€¯ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€«ğ‘€¸ğ‘€­ğ‘€˜ğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€”ğ‘€¼ğ‘€ ğ‘€…ğ‘€¤ğ‘€²ğ‘†ğ‘€²ğ‘€¦ğ‘€ ğ‘€•ğ‘€¢ğ‘„ ğ‘€§ğ‘€¸ğ‘€§ğ‘€ºğ‘€«ğ‘€¢ğ‘„. ğ‘€¢ğ‘€ºğ‘€¡ğ‘†ğ‘€¡ğ‘„ ğ‘€®ğ‘„ğ‘€“ğ‘‚ ğ‘€¯ğ‘€ºğ‘€²ğ‘€¢ğ‘†ğ‘€¢ğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘€ ğ‘€¯ğ‘€ºğ‘€²ğ‘†ğ‘€²ğ‘€¢ğ‘†ğ‘€£ğ‘„ ğ‘€•ğ‘€˜ğ‘†ğ‘€™ğ‘€¢ğ‘€º, ğ‘€¯ğ‘€ºğ‘€²ğ‘†ğ‘€²ğ‘€¢ğ‘†ğ‘€£ğ‘„ ğ‘€¢ğ‘€ºğ‘€ğ‘†ğ‘€ğ‘€¢ğ‘€º, ğ‘€¯ğ‘€ºğ‘€²ğ‘†ğ‘€²ğ‘€¢ğ‘†ğ‘€£ğ‘„ ğ‘€¦ğ‘€ºğ‘€²ğ‘€»ğ‘€¤ğ‘€¢ğ‘€º, ğ‘€¯ğ‘€ºğ‘€²ğ‘†ğ‘€²ğ‘€¢ğ‘†ğ‘€£ğ‘„ ğ‘€²ğ‘‚ğ‘€¬ğ‘†ğ‘€¬ğ‘€ ğ‘€“ğ‘€§ğ‘†ğ‘€§ğ‘‚ğ‘€¢ğ‘€º. ğ‘€¢ğ‘€ ğ‘€“ğ‘€ºğ‘€²ğ‘†ğ‘€² ğ‘€³ğ‘‚ğ‘€¢ğ‘€¼? ğ‘€…ğ‘€¦ğ‘€¸ğ‘€§ğ‘€¸ğ‘€£ğ‘€•ğ‘€¢ğ‘„, ğ‘€ªğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€”ğ‘€¯ğ‘‚, ğ‘€§ğ‘€¸ğ‘€§ğ‘€ºğ‘€«ğ‘€¢ğ‘„â€ğ‘€¢ğ‘€º.

1006\. ğ‘€‡ğ‘€¤ğ‘€«ğ‘€¯ğ‘„ğ‘€˜ ğ‘€ªğ‘€•ğ‘€¯ğ‘€¸. ğ‘€…ğ‘€¢ğ‘†ğ‘€¢ğ‘€«ğ‘€¦ğ‘€¸ ğ‘€¢ğ‘‚ ğ‘€ªğ‘€ºğ‘€“ğ‘†ğ‘€”ğ‘€½ ğ‘€ªğ‘€•ğ‘€¯ğ‘€¢ğ‘„ ğ‘€ªğ‘€¸ğ‘€²ğ‘€ºğ‘€¢ğ‘€ ğ‘€…ğ‘€ªğ‘€ºğ‘€¦ğ‘€¦ğ‘†ğ‘€¤ğ‘€¼ğ‘€¦ğ‘†ğ‘€¢ğ‘€º.
